MEXICO
TRADE POLICY LOAN
Loan and Project Sum_ary
Borrower:      Banco Nacional de Comercio Exter ,or, S.N.C.
Guarantor:     United Mexican States
ABONnt:         US$500.0 million equivalent
Terms:          15 years, including three years of grace, at the standard
variable interest rate
Project
Description:   The proposed loan would support the Government's long-term
objective of gradually opening the Mexican economy through
implementation of trade reforms, as a means of improving domes-
tic efficiency and international competitiveness. The loan may
be seen as part of a larger program of Bank financial and tech-
nical support for Mexico's 1986-88 trade liberalization program,
including one or two possible follow-up Trade Policy Loans
(TPLs), a follow-up Export Development Project, and one or more
possible industrial reconversion projects to assist both private
and public sector firms in their adjustment to a more open trade
policy environment. The proposed loan would also serve to
assist Mexico in obtaining associated external financing from
commercial banks and other sources. Specifically, this loan
vould support the reduction of Non-Tariff Barriers (NTBa), which
have historically constituted the heart of the Mexican system of
protection and the simplification and reduced dispersion of
tariffs. The loan would finance part of the additional imports
resulting from the removal of NTBs. After an adjustment period
which will include further import liberalization measures,
additional non-oil export growth resulting from the trade policy
reforms should more than compensate for the additional imports.
The benefits of the program would be an improvement in the
balance of payments, the gradual recovery of sustainable growth
and employment based on productivity and efficiency gains, and
the strengthening of external confidence and creditworthiness.
Project
Risks:         A primary risk of the project relates to the danger of a macro
policy framework inconsistent with a gradual and sustainable
opening of the economy. A second major risk is the danger that
NTBs will not be reduced sufficiently quickly to establish the
full credibility of the three-year tariff reduction program.
- v -



The existence of still pervasive NTIs could disturb the incen-
tive effects of the tariff regime and weaken the inducement on
the part of producers to adjust to a more economy-policy envi-
ronment, Other risks relate to the length and difficulty of the
adjustment process; domestic pressures against the conitinued
implementation of the trade policy reform program; delays in
implementing complementary reforms in areas such as agriculture,
the parastatal sector, and the financial system; and uncertain-
ties In the external environment. These risks are reduced by
the Government's strong commitment to trade policy reform, as
demonstrated by the significant reductions in quantitative
import restrictions in 1985. Mexico's decision to seek GATT
membership and to negotiate a new program with the IMF also
reduces the risk of incomplete implementation of trade policy
reforms or backsliding. There is also a risk that effectiveness
of the loan may be delayed because of continuing uncertainty
about the availability of sufficient net additional external
financing for 1986 from other sources.
Eatimated
Disbursemeuts:       The loan would be disbursed in two tranches.  The first
tranche of US$300 million would be available for disburse-
ment at the time of loan effectiveness, including receipt
by the Bank of satisfactory assurances that an appropriate
financing package will be obtained from other lenders,
including commercial banks and export credit agencies. The
second tranche of US$189 million would be available upon
fulfillment of conditions for second tranche release, which
include the further reduction of NTBs by a minimum of 5% as
measured by the coverage of domestic production. If the
latter condition is not met by December 31, 1986, the
second tranche will be cancelled, unless otherwise agreed
between the Bank and Mexico. Disbursements of the entire
loan, including US$3 million for technical studies and US$8
million for the establishment of a modern antidumping
system, would be completed before November 30, 1988.

REPORT MD RBECONENDATION OF TS  PRSIDENT OF TEE IERD
TO THE EECUTIVE DIR ORS 01
A PROPOSED LOAN TO BMCO NhCIONALDE CMODECW  EXTERIOR, S.N.C.
WITr lTHE WUARNTEE OF UNITED  XXICAI STATES
FOR A TRADE POLICY WAN
1.     I submit the following report and recommendation on a proposed loan to
Banco Nacional de Coinercio Exterior, S.N.C. (BANCOMEXT), with the guarantee of
United Mexican States, for the equivalent of US$500 million, in support of the
Government's program of trade policy reform. The loan would be repaid over 15
years, including 3 years of grace, at the standard variable interest rate.
PART I - TS ECONOMY UNTIL TEe CRISIS 0 1982
2.      An economic report, entitled "Mexico: Recent Economic Developments and
Prospects," was issued May 7, 1984 (4996-ME). A new Country Economic Memoran-
dum is scheduled to be issued by August 1986.   Country data are provided in
Annex I.
A.     INTRODUCTION
3.      The growth performance of Mexico over the three decades after 1950 is
among the best in the Western Hemisphere, at an annual average GDP growth rate
of 6.4%. But these averages obscure the Increasingly costly and unsustainable
growth strategies pursued and the rising instability of the Mexican economy
over the period. Shortly after World War II, Mexico adopted an import-substi-
tution strategy to industrialize a largely rural economy.   Between 1940 and
1970, agriculture's share in the economy fell from 212 to 11%, while industry's
grew from 25% to 34%.  Protection of new i1tdustries was intended to be tempo-
rary, but instead protection continually inc:eased and left the Mexican economy
unable  to compete in world trade.   The loris of competitiveness was further
aggravated after the oil boom of the 1970s.
B      DIFOIT-SUBSTITJTINC INDUSTEIALIZAIION, 1947-70
4.     During nearly two decades, between 1947 and the mid-1960s, increasingly
protected domestic markets provided a stimulus to private sector investment
and growth. The public sector retained a relatively modest share in economic
activity--on average about 15% of GDP (Figure 1)--awarding diverse incentives.
usually on a discretionary basis, to private investment. Through high Import
tariffs and a complex system of import licensing, domestic Industry was
protected from competition from imports--theoretically as a "temporary" support
for "infant industries," although in practice indefinitely.  Monetary and ex-
change rate stability further supported financial deepening and growth.
5.     By the late 1960s, the relatively easy and efficient opportunities for
import substitution had been exhausted. With the rising anti-export bias ema-
nating from the trade regime, Mexico's share in world exports had declined



- 2 -
steadily from 0.9Z in 1948-50 to 0.5% by 1968-70 (Figure 2).   With seports
declining rapidly as a share of GDP (Figure 3), preferential imports of needed
capital goods and raw materials generated chronic trade deficits. The bias in
favor of local manufacturing was compounded by the provision of cheap capital
imports, cheap energy, and other public utility inputs.  These policies were
heavily biased against labor and labor-intensive sectors, in a country whose
population was growing at an annual rate of over 32 during the 1960s and where
a large proportion  of people was  (and  is)  living  in poverty.   Increasing
unemployment, formal or unrecorded, was one result; very low wages for
non-governmental and non-union labor, another; and large-scale outmigration,
yet another.

6o.    Mexico faced a strategic policy choice in the late 1960s between movlng
to an internationally-competitive growth path, or deepening its commitment to
inward-oriented development. A gradual opening of the economy would at that
tilme have been possible at limited cost because import substitution had been
focussed primarily on consumer goods, In which Mexico could have readily
achieved international competitiveness.  But Mexico's import-substituting pri-
vate sector (including foreign-owned Eirms producing for the domestic market)
refused to be deprived of lts captive domestic market. arguing successfully
that reduction of protectionlot barriers would destroy local lndustry. A large
Government bureaucracy similarly opposed the elimination of discretionary con-
trols on  foreign  trade.   Mexico  twice--in 1971-72 and  again  in  1977-78--
attempted and then reversed trade llberalizatlon programs.  For the 15 years
from 1970 to 1985, Mexico thus postponed needed structural reforms and opted
instead for a deepening of its protectionist and inward-looking development
path.
7.      With an untenable external trade imbalance and no productivity growth
after 1970, the maintenance of an gnward-oroented dncentive regite foremlosed
opportunities for sustainable growth ant employment based on comparative advan-
tage and international tracet   Protected smport-substitutm on was ubs      tensiin ed



- I -
and  extended  down  the chain of  production  to many  Intermediate  and capital
goods, for which Mexico did not have the domestic market scale necessary to
achieve efficient productlon without exporting.  Mexico conceived of Itself Am
one of the largest economies in the developing world, ignoring the fact that,
in terms of effective purchasing power, its internal market was no larger than
that of London or Los Angeles.   Mexico's effective market size for non-petro-
leum-related  industrial products 1s less than  one-tenth of that of countries
such as France or Germany, which rely on International specialliation to ensure
efficient  production;  yet  Mexico  progressively  Isolated  itself  from  world
markets, with non-oil exports declining steadily to less than 32 of CDP by 1980
(Figure 3).  This is one of the lowest export-GDP ratios of any economy in the
world--lndustrialized or developing (Table 1)--and It made the Mexican economy
unusually vulnerable to external sector shocks.

8.     In the absence of productivity gains since the late 1960's, growth since
1970 has been contrived, first via public expenditure-led growth (1971-76),
financed largely through foreign borrowing, and subsequently (1978-82) via
booming oil revenues and additional foreign borrowing, made possible by the
prospect of future income from oil.  By the time both of these options had been
exhausted, Mexico by the mid-1980. faced an external imbalance touch greater
than that of 1970, an incentive regime much more severely distorted, and an
economy  even  less  able  to compete  internationally.   The  recent collapse  in
world oil prices now creates an imperative for Mexico to move forward urgently
with the structural reforms that have been postponed since the late 1960a.
These reforms are dictated not primarily by the fall in oil prices--because
Mexico can continue to produce oil profitably at prices even below those cur-
rently prevailing in world markets (about US$12-$14 per barrel) nor are they
dictated by Mexico's need to service the interest on its foreign debt, which
continues to be payable in its entirety out of net PEMEX export revetiues alone
(thus leaving the net balance approximately unchanged vis-a-vis the payments
situation prior to the surge in foreign borrowing after the late 1970s).
Rather,  they  are  dictated  by  the  need  to  recover  productivity  growth  and
employment.

9.       Fortunately, Mexico has a record of remarkable export responsiveness
whenever exchange rate and other policies have been appropriate.  For example,
between 1970 and 1973, manufactured exports (including net exports of the in-
bond sector) increased from US$444 million to US$1,313 million--a real annual
growth of 34%.   Again,  between 1976 and 1979, overall manufactured exports
increased from US$2.0 billion to US$3.4 billion, for a real annual growth rate
of 8%.  Most recently,  in the period 1982-1985, overall manufactured exports
rose from US$3.9 billion to US$6.4 billion, despite some decline in 1985, for
an average annual growth rate of 15% in real terms.   Moreover, the in-bond
sector--which benefits from a relatively unencumbered free-trade status--has
had an impressive growth performance over the years, despite increasing insta-
bility oE exchange rate and other policies. Between 1970 and 1982, net exports
of the in-bond sector alone rose from US$83 million to US$851 million, and
since 1982 these have risen by a further 55% to US$1.3 billion. Employment in
the in-bond sector has grown rapidly since 1982, far outstripping the overall
rate of employment growth in the economy as a whole.  In response to the large
real devaluation of the Mexican peso since mid-1985 (Figure 4), non-oil exports
are again booming, up by 20% in real terms during the first four months of 1986
over the like period of 1985.
C.     PUBLIC EZPENDITURE-LED GROWTH, 1971-1976
10.     Protect.on from foreign competition and the confinement of production
primarily to domestic markets, not only prevented industry from gaining in pro-
ductivity and the efficiency to compete abroad, but also penalized the primary
sectors (especially agriculture and mining) that had previously been export-
oriented, by skewing incentives and drawing private sectur resources away from
these sectors.  Virtually all sectors became increasingly dependent on fiscal
incentives, subsidized input prices, preferential credit, and "buy Mexico"
procurement policies of the public sector.   By 1970,  federal non-oil taxes
accounted for an extremely low 7.6% of GDP (Figure 5), while subsidies and
transfer payments alone accounted for about 5% of GDP (Figure 6).   But the
private sector also resisted efforts to strengthen Government finances and to
increase taxes and social expenditures to provide for a rapidly growing
population.

11.     By 1971-72, both trade and tax reform initiatLves had failed, and the
Government embarked on a rapid expansion of public sector expenditure to ensure
continued economic growth and employment. The number of companies with Govern-
ment participatlon more than doubled, to 845, between 1970 and 1976. Parastat-
al enterprises expanded faster than the economy at large, relying increasingly
on transfers from the Federal Government budget.
12.    After years in which inflation in Mexico had closely followed trends in
world inflation, expansionary spending policies initiated a process of rising
inflation (Figure 7).  By 1975-76,  the consolidated public sector deficit had
reached nearly 10% of GDP (Figure 8), and inflation was in excess of 15%.
Financial  deepening  was  reversed  (Figure  9),  and  capital  flight  ensued
(Figure 10). Crowding out of the private sector in the domestic credit markets
intensified (Figure 11), despite the financing of the balance of payments
through rapidly growing foreign borrowing by the public sector (Figure 12).
The share of the public sector in GDP, which had remained below 20% prior to
1970, rose to 28% by 1976 (Figure 1).

13.     Basic incentive-regime distortions that were present before 1970 were
exacerbated by inflationary policies, an overvalued exchange rate, and negative
interest rates. Quantitative restrictions vere raised back to record new lev-
els (Figure 15). Mexico tbus incurred the combined effects of a long misdirec-
ted growth strategy and increasingly inappropriate macropolicy.  The economy's
long period of stab:lity and private sector confidence were undermined. Real
economic growth averaged 6.2X over the period 1970-76, but it was contrived in
an inward-oriented economy on the strength of an unsustainable rise in public
expenditure and foreign debt.
D.     GROWTH THROUGH OIL AND FOREIGN BORRnWILMGw 1977-1982
14.     Following the 1976 crisis, the Government undertook a combined program
of stabilization and trade liberalization.  There was a first phase to disman-
tle import controls by 1980, to be followed by a gradual lowering of tariffs.

There were renewed efforts at tax reform, protection reduction, and stimulation
of non-oil exports.  Mexico obtained a three-year facility from the IMF, and
negotiated a highly favorable pact for Mexico to enter GATT.  A second year of
slow growth in 1977 followed on major new oil discoveries (Figure 13), that
came to be seen as the means to finance a quick return to high growth, without
politically costly structural reforms. By 1978, no further drawings took place
under the IMF program; and two years later, under combined pressure from busi-
ness and labor, the Government decided against joining GATT.  Modest progress
in trade liberalization was reversed after 1980, with QRs rising quickly to new
highs by 1982 (Figure 15).

15.     Proven oil reserves boomed (Figure 13), and so did the world price of
oil (Figure 14). This double oil boom served as a lever for enlarged borrowing
to finance expanding public investment and other expenditures; and the Govern-
ment also encouraged private firms to seek foreign credits against Mexico's
prospect of future oil revenues.   Net external borrowing by the public sector
(Figure 12) totalled US$34.5 billion over the period 1979-82, compared to total
investment in the petroleum sector alone of some US$30 billion over the same
period.    Public  expenditure,  including  by  parastatal  enterprises,  nearly
doubled in real terms between 1977 and 1982, rising from 28% of GDP in 1976 to
a new high of 42.5% of GDP by 1982 (Figure 1).  The real exchange rate appre-
ciated by more than 25% (Figure 4), and the public external debt jumped to 30%
of GDP, from 18% in 1976.  Moreover, as domestic crowding out of the private
sector accelerated once again (Figure 11), the private sector's foreign debt
increased from US$4.9 billion in 1976 to US$20 billion by mid-1982.
16.     Real growth averaged a high 6.1% again during the period 1976-82.  But
growth and investment patterns, financed by oil and foreign debt, moved Mexico
still further away from its international comparative advantages, thus deep-
ening the structural misdirection of the economy that began in the late 1940s.
Large-scale public and private investments with low international rates of
return, such as steel and many capital goods, were undertaken or encouraged



- 9 -
behind high and rising effective protection barriers (Annex VII).  To offset
the growing inefficiencies and rigidities of a protected economy, an ever
increaeing share of resources went into subsidies of all kinds--up from the
aforementioned  5% of GDP in  1970 to 8.5%  by  1980  (Figure  6).   Overall
subsidies, measured on an economic rather than a purely budgetary basis,
reached 14% of GDP by 1980 (Table 2).

17.    The fiscal deficit reached 14.72 of GDP in 1981, and 17.6% by 1982.  The
Government devalued in 1982 by 268Z in nominal terms; nationalized the banking
system and 467 bank-owned firms; imposed foreign exchange controls and forcible
conversion of 'Mex-dollar" deposits; and suspended principal payments on the
US$60 billion foreign debt. Capital flight over the period 1976-82 is estimat-
ed to have reached a cumulative total of US$29 billion (Figure 10); and the
size of the domestic financial system bad by 1982 reached a new post-1977 low
(Figure 9).   Non-oil export shares  fell  to new lows during  this  period
(Figure 3), as did foreign reserve levels (Figure 16). Quantitative restric-
tions (QRs) were extended to 100% of imports.  The crisis of 1982 resulted
essentially from fiscal overexpansion in an economy with a severely repressed
export capability.
PART II - THE FIRST PHASE OF ADJUSTMENT. 1983-85
A.     EXASTlION OF GR0TH:  1983-85
18.     Stabilization via Demand-Manageent.  Beginning in late 1982, with the
support of a new Extended Fund Facility from the IMF, Mexico undertook a stabi-
lization program based on drastic contraction of douestic demand, through fis-
cal, exchange rate, and monetary policy. The exchange rate was depreciated to

an unprecedented new low in real terms (Figure 4).   Import controls had been
tightened before the program with the Fund was started. The program called for
a dismantling of those controls, a process that war to be initiated after the
devaluation of Decpmber 1982.   Public secLor expenditure  in relation to GDP
declined over the period 1983-85, from the high of 42.52 in 1982 to about 332
by 1985 (Figure 1).  Won-interest expenditure declined to about 222 of GDP by
1985, a 10-year low (Figure 17).  Public sector investment was cut from about
82 of GDP in the late 1970s to only 52, again a 10-year low (Figure 18).  Non-
interest current expenditure also declined, though more modestly, and remained
still at the levels of the late 1970s.  Real wages fell 252-352 between 1982
and 1984 (Figure 19).   Most public sector prices were raied  in real  terms.
Public sector savings, adjusted for the inflation component of intsrest pay-
ments, reached record highs (Figure 20), and similarly adjusted deficits
reached new lows (Figure 21).  Monetary growth of domestic origin became con-
tractionary (Figure 22), allowing the foreign reserve cover for the monetary
base to recover its level of the mid-1970s, albeit a level still well below
those observed during the period of monetary stability prior to 1970 (Figure
16).
19.      The results of the stabilization effort for 1983-84 were  impressive.
Imports fell 222, non-oil exports rDse 622, and the resource balance showvd
enormous and unprecedented  surpluses  in 1983-84  (Figure 23),  reflecting  the
high  surplus  in  the  "primary"  (non-interest)  fiscal accounts  (Figure 27).
Despite declining oil prices, net foreign reserves increased from a negative
US$2.0 billion to US$6.5 billion between the end of 1982 and the end oE 1984.
The 1983-84 performance was hailed by the international financial community as
a model for other countries.  A massive foreign debt restructuring was success-
fully negotiated.  Total net foreign borrowing for the three-year period 1983-
85 amounted to US$4 billion, for an actual decline in the overall debt, in real
terms, of about 1%.  The EFF, which had been framed in a medium-term context,
called for important structural reforms, including a reduction of the size of
the public sector and the liberalization of the exchange and trade regimes.
But, unfortunately, the implementation of the liberalization effort was very
slow during 1983-84.   The optimistic reaction to Nexico's success in 1983-84
overLooked the short-term emphasis of the stabilization effort and the slowness
in implementing  long overdue structural reforms.   Optimism turned  to dispn-
chantment 18 months later, and external creditors came to recognize that sta-
bilization in the absence of more fundamental structural reEorms could not
restore Mexico's former levels of creditworthiness nor would sustainable
economic growth be possible without suchi reforms.

20.     Growth throuLh Internal BorrwXLi.  Despite drastic fiscal and monetary
contraction, inflation in both 1983 and 1984 turned out to be much higher than
had been projected by the authorities and assumed in the IMF program. The fis-
cal deficit fell substantially in 1983 (to 8.5% of GDP), but then levelled off
in 1984. GDP fell in both 1982 and 1983, and recovered only partially in 1984
(Figure 24). Consumption per capita by 1984 remained below the levels of 1980
(Figure 25), as the Government prepared for the mid-term Congressional elec-
tions of July 1985.   By late 1984,  fiscal policy began to ease, and public
sector borrowing incroased substantially. Monetary policy turned less restric-
tive, the exchange rate once again appreciated in real terms (see Figure 4),
and relatively small deviations from the program led to a renewed surge of
inflation. The recovery of private sector investment was affected by uncer-
tainties over the handling of the external debt that had been accumulated by
the private sector in the period 1978-82 and by the delays in removing exchange
and trade restrictions.  With the introduction of a new scheme to restructure
the private sector external debt (FICORCA), the situation improved, and by
mid-1984 there was a stronger-than-expected recovery of the private sector.
21.     Most program targets were missed in 1985.  The fiscal deficit rose to
9.8%; inflation increased to 63.8%; non-oil exports duclined from their 1984
levels; and most components of the balance of payments deteriorated.   Foreign
reserves  declined  by  nearly  US$3.5  billion.    While  external  public  debt
declined  in real terms during 1985,  the domestic debt of the public sector
surged back up toward the level of 1982 (Figure 26). As a result, crowding out
of the private sector in the domestic credit markets was severe, as the share
of the public sector in total credit reached an unprecedented high in excess of
75% in 1985 (Figure 11).  The brief spurt of GDP growth between mid-1984 and
mid-1985 came quickly to a halt in late 1985, and prospects for 1986 are for
another decline in real GUP.

B.     EVALUATION OF THE 1983-85 POLICY PERFORMNCE
22.     An Inappropriate Policy Mix.  The failure of the 1985 program has se-
verely undermined the confidence at home and abroad that was returning during
the 1983-84 period.  But considered more closely,  it is clear that the failure
of the 1983-85 stabilization program was due not primarily to a lack of effort
on the macropolicy side, but to an inappropriate mix of aggressive demand-
management policies and very slow progress in the Government's original plans
to move simultaneously in expanding aggregate supply through improvements in
the productivity and efficiency of the economy.  The lack of structural reform,
particularly the failure to liberalize trade, left the economy bottled up
inside high protective barriers, vulnerable to wide swings in response to small
deviations of policy and without opportunities for growth on a sustainable
basis.
23.     The failure to move on trade liberalization worked in several ways to
frustrate the effects of contractionary macropolicy throughout the period 1983-
85. The stabilization effort was itself faulty for not explicitly recognizing
that the macro policy and inflation targets could not be met without the imple-
mentation of congruent structural measures envisioned in the original program,
especially trade liberalization, and for not recognizing the unusual sensi-
tivity of the budget and other variables to excess inflation.  Mexico, unlike
many other Latin American countries, has had limited experience vith high
inflation.   It was,  therefore,  a central objective of policy over the past
three years to return to low inflation and resist all forms of indexation--of
wages, interest rates, public pricing, the exchange rate--as potentially desta-
bilizing. But the failure to move decisively to free the system from trade and
exchange restrictions reduced the effectiveness of the efforts to reduce
inflation.   With continued high levels of trade restrictiveness,  the economy
remained vulnerable to small errors in macro policy, and the relaxation of the
adjustment effort in 1985 resulted in a resurgence of inflationary pressures.
24.     The massive 50% devaluation of the real exchange rate in 1982-83 might
have been consistent and tenable with a full and immediate elimination of
import barriers.   But import controls were not sufficiently relaxed  in that
period, contrary to original intentions.  Thus, the real exchange rate in the
period 1982-83 turned out to be initially undervalued (Figure 4), and hence
inflationary,  even with a draconian fiscal effort.   The monetary authorities
could have targetted the real exchange rate and accepted whatever inflation
rate that might imply, or alternatively an even greater fiscal contraction.
But the presumption of the stabilization program was that inflation must and
would be brought down and that trade liberalization would proceed expeditiously
to support that objective.  The authorities recognized that an indexed "crawl"
might temporarily sustain the greatly undervalued exchange rate, but at the
risk of an explosive wage-inflation-devaluation spiral that would be politi-
cally untenable.
25.    Second, the fiscal targets were formulated and implemented on a consoli-
dated domestic and foreign currency basis, ill-suited to a closed economy in
which the flow of goods and services, at any given exchange rate, was con-
strained by high import restrictions, that led to a transformation problem.
The domestic monetization of the foreign currency surplus (Figure 27), to
finance part of the domestic currency deficit, created a claim on external



- 15 -
resources that could not translate readily into effective domestic aggregate
demand for imports, and was thus simply inflationary. The real rosource equiv-
alent of the foreign currency surplus was blocked by high import barriers and
the initially undervalued exchange rate of 1982-83.   In the absence of major
import liberalization in 1983-84, the only  feasible adjustment to effect the
real transfer of the foreign currency surplus was an appreciation of the real
exchange rate.   The monetary authorities correctly perceived  in 1983-84 that
maintaining a highly undervalued exchange rate in this environment would lead
to continued reserve accumulation and would only further accelerate Inflation.
The need for more effective fiscal and monetary management of Mexico's oil
revenues thus further accentuates the importance of trade liberalizat ion.
26.     The single factor that helps most to account for the disappointing out-
come in 1983-85, despite major domestic effort, is the large weight of interest
payments on the domestic public debt and other inflation-sensitive components
of public expenditure, such as pricing and interest rate subsidies (Figure 6).
Once the dynamics of excess inflation were in place, for the reasons discussed
in the previous paragraphs, the result was that these inflation-linked compo-
nents of public expenditure continually exceeded expectations and caused ex-
penditure overruns not directly subject  to public policy control.   Interest
payments on the domestic debt alone accounted for 6.3% of GDP, on average, over
the 1983-85 period.  The unforeseen excess-over-budget of these payments alone
accounts for most of the fiscal deficit overruns for the period. These higher
deficits meant higher nominal public borrowing requirements, even though the
operational and primary deficits were low by historical standards (Figures
26-36).
27.     The Failure of a Sustained zpgort Response.  The implementation in 1983
of a "free-trade status" for exporters, with the support of the World Bank in
the form of the first Export Development Project, substantially diminished the
anti-export bias of the trade regime, but the response of non-oil exports faded
after the 62% increase in the 1983-84  period.   As QRs rather than tariffs
provided the effective constraint on most imports, there was no ceiling on the
level of effective protection to domestic sales as against competition from
abroad.   Domestic prices of tradable goods rose even faster than the general
price level during most of the period 1983-85.   As domestic demand recovered
from the depressed levels of 1983, effective protection to domestic sales thus
rose pani passu, given that QRs rather than tariffs were the primary trade
policy instrument; and hence so did the resource pull away from export markets.
28.      Even though imports by exporters were free of both QRs and tariffs,
under the 1983 "free-trade regime" for exporters, domestic sales remained high-
ly protected and hence relatively more profitable during this period. In addi-
tion, the real exchange rate appreciation of 1984-85 (Figure 4) further eroded
the international competitiveness  of non-oil exports.   As a result of these
various factors, non-oil exports declined steadily after the first quarter of
1984, despite the implementation of the very liberal 1983 import regime for
exporters.
29.     The Failure to Strengthen Access to Voluntary Lending.  After its uni-
lateral decision to suspend payments on its external debt in 1982, Mexico in
the period 1983-85 obtained the largest debt restructuring ever undertaken (in

the amount of USD49 billion), on the *xpectation--uhared by the Government and
Its foreign creditors--that debt relief would enable Mexico to stabilize its
economy and bogin the dooper structural reforms needed in order for Mexico to
achieve sustainable long-term growth.  By late 1985, it war clear that little
progress had boon achieved in structural reforms and that Mexico haed not recov-
ered Che external creditworthinoes and confidence needed to return as planned
to voluntary landing by 1986. Mexico projected late In 1985 that it would face
large new financing requirements in 1986, even before the oil price decline of
early 1986, and it was not expected that theme would be met through a return to
normal voluntary commercial lending.   This already difficult outlook was fur-
ther aggravated by the fall in world oil prices and Mexico's loss of export
volume (primarily the result of uncompetitive pricing policiem).
Co     THE START OF GENERAL TRADE L  IRALUZATION:  1985 1/
30.    At the start of 1985 approximately 83% of imports and 100% of domestic
production were subject to QRs.  In early 1985, the Government announced its
lntention to reduce QRs to between 55Z and 652 of imports by the end of 1985.
That objective became part of the program agreed with the IMF. But only limit-
ed trade liberalizatLon was achleved In the first half of 1985, against strong
opposition in many parts of the private sector and the Administration.  How-
ever, a major bilateral trade accord was reached with tho United States, which
committed Mexico to phase out remaining export subsidies, in return for getting
a GATT-style  Injury clause  In the application of countervailing  duties on
Mexican exports to the U.S.
31.     Partly in response to the persistence of high inflation, the Government
on July 25 abruptly eliminated QRs from 3,604 tariff positions, out of a total
of 8,100.  This brought the ratio of controlled lmports down to 39X of import
value, the lowest level in a quarter century (Figure 4).  Nearly 45X of pro-
duction thus was freed.  The July 25 measures had a strong impact on expecta-
tions because of the unprecedented sweep and speed with which they were carried
out.   This contrasted markedly with the previous liberalizatlon attempts in
1970-71 and 1977-80 (see Part I), whlch had involved a very slow reduction of
QRs that became administratively unmanageable because it was discretionary and
lacked transparency, thus permitting Interest groups to exert pressure against
the removal of QRs.
32.     As part of the July 25 reforms, the authorities undertook a series of
adjustments in the structure of tariffs to avoid an abrupt and potentially dis-
ruptive shift in the level and structure of effective protection. Over several
decades, during which QRs had been the primary instrument of protection, tar-
iffs had been a relatively inactive and subsidiary instrument of protection.
The level and dispersion of tariffs were adjusted in response to the overvalua-
tion of the exchange rate prior to 1982 and were gradually lowered after 1983.
As part of the July 25 measures, average overall tariffs increased marginally
from 23.4% to 25.4%, while dispersion declined from 25.5% in 1984 to 18.91
after July 25, 1985 (Figure 31).
I/    For a more detailed discussion of the 1985 trade reforms, see Annex VI.

33.     Antidumplng protection, which had in the past been possible via the
direct use of QRs, became a matter of increased concern. During the first half
of 1985, the coverage of official import reference prices (ORPs) had been
reduced considerably from 1,348 tariff positions at end-1984, accounting for
10% of imports of that year, to 4.4% by July 1, 1985.  In the wake of the QR
reduction on July 25 and some minor additional QR reductions in the months
thereafter, the authorities once again expanded the use of ORPs as a defense
against dumping to 7.6% of 1984 imports. Present ORP coverage (as of April 30,
1986) has been reduced to 1,171 positions, representing 7.0% of 1984 imports.
The estimated equivalent of domestic production covered by ORPs is at present
about 17.5%,2/ of which about 5.0% is also covered by QRs.  At the same time,
the authorities submitted new antidumping legislation to Congress; indicated
that future antidumping policy would be based on GATT-style countervailing
duties rather than distortionary ORPs; and announced that all ORPs would be
phased out over a two-year transitional period and eliminated by end-1987.
34.    Mexico's Neigotations with CATT.  The dramatic liberalization of trade
on July 25 made it possible for Mexico to once again consider entry into GATT.
In November 1985, the Mexican Senate authorized President de la Madrid to ini-
tiate negotiations for Mexico's accession into GATT.   That same month Mexico
formally presented its application to GATT, receiving a very favorable initial
response.  Formal negotiations began in February 1986, and the Government hopes
that these will be concluded by September 1986 so that Mexico can participate
as a full member in the forthcoming new GATT round. As of mid-1986, it appears
that Mexico will successfully conclude its negotiations for accession on sched-
ule, and on terms that could possibly place Mexico in the front ranks of LDC
membership in GATT, as measured by its commitment to enter into early compli-
ance not only with the general principles of GATT but also with several of key
GATT codes to which many LDCs have not yet subscribed.
2/     The methodology developed for measuring the production-weighted coverage
of NTBs has been agreed to in the Memorandum on Methodology between the
Government and the Bank (Annex V).



- 19 -
PART III - THE SECOND PHASE OF ADJUSTMENT, 1986-88
A.     MEDIUM-TERN OBJECTIVES
35.    The Mexican authorities have recognized that a renewed demand-management
effort will be needed in 1986 to recover internal and external balance and move
on to a sustainable growth path by the late 19809 (see Annex IV). But in addi-
tion, beginning with the trade policy reforms of July 1985 and the decision to
seek entry into GATT, the Government has accelerated the implementation of the
original objectives of the comprehensive National Development Plan of 1982,
particularly that of a gradual opening of the economy.
36.     The medium-term prospects remain difficult.   The recently accelerated
decline in world oil prices will substantially reduce the Government's ability
to subsidize continued domestic inefficiency, thus requiring more decisive and
rapid structural adjustments.   The reduced margin of oil export revenue over
external interest payments implies that the attainment of an overall balance of
payments equilibrium and declining inflation will require determined efforts to
constrain domestic absorption, while gradually opening the economy over the
next  several years.   As already noted,  structural adjustments  are needed--
irrespective of the price of oil and the level of the debt service--in order
for Mexico to recover sustainable growth of income and employment through
productivity growth rather than subsidies.
37.    The change in the international oil market situation is having a signi-
ficant  impact on the Mexican economy in 1986.   Oil export earnings in 1985
represented about half of Mexico's total exports of goods and services, and
similarly about one-half of total public sector revenues. Each US$1 per barrel
drop in international oil prices can be expected to reduce Mexico's revenues by
about US$600 million, assuming no change in the volume of oil production and
exports.
B.     THE N&CEOPOLICY FRAHENORK FOR 1986-88
38.     The macropolicy framework and external financing plan for 1986-88 are
crucial to the viability of the trade liberalization process that would be sup-
ported by the proposed TPL. Throughout the Bank's discussions with the Govern-
ment regarding Mexico's plan to redirect the country's economic development
strategy toward outward-oriented growth--including the appraisal and negotia-
tion of the proposed TPL, there has been a shared understanding that an appro-
priate macro policy framework would be essential to achieve the objectives of
the loan. The credibility of the Bank's support for trade liberalization would
be at risk, just as would be that of the Government itself, if highly visible
commitments were to be made to trade liberalization without assurances of a
supportive structure of macro policy, in particular, fiscal, exchange rate, and
interest rate policies.   For this reason, the proposed TPL provides that nei-
ther tranche will be disbursed except as the Bank is satisfied that the macro
policy framework is consistent with the Government's trade liberalization
objectives.



- 20 -
39.      As discussed in Part II of this Report, Mexico undertook major macro
policy adjustments during the stabilization phase that followed the crisis of
1982.   The stabilization phase of 1983-84 was effective  in bringing about a
needed contraction in overall domestic absorption and in generating substantial
external surpluses (see Figure 23). But, as noted in Part II.B, the policy mix
was heavily skewed toward demand management and austerity measures, with only
slow progress In implementing the equally pressing structural reforms that
wouLd raise productivity and increase supply along with the contraction in
duiiiand. The result was a severe recession, a major decline in real wages, and
growing political pressures for a shift to m more expansionary macro policy,
even though underlying conditions of the economy still did not permit a
sustainable resumption of growth and employment.
40.    During the TPL appraisal and negotiation process, the Government's poli-
cy program for 1986-88 underwent a series of changes, partly in response to the
reemergence of fiscal imbalance and inflationary pressures in 1985 and to the
collapse of oil prices during early 1986.  The Bank's appraisal team undertook
extensive analysis of each new program, including the most recent one, as
refLected  in the Government's Statement of Foreign Trade Policy of June 19
(reproduced in Annex IV).  However, there were doubts as to whether the fiscal
effort implied in that Statement would be sufficient to forestall intensifica-
tion of inflationary pressures.   Moreover,  considering the Government's con-
tinued efforts to adjust to these adverse circumstances and its ongoing nego-
tiations with the IHF, the June 19 policy program may be revised further in the
coming weeks.   It is for this reason that no detailed analysis of the June 19
policy program is provided in this document.  At the time of Board presenta-
tion, the staff will provide to the Board an explanation and assessment of the
policy program as it will have evolved up to that date.
41.     The current economic situation,  despite significant fiscal effort and
adjustments of the exchange rate, interest rates, and credit policies, has
deteriorated seriously since the collapse of international oil prices in early
1986. Without continued domestic adjustments, particularly to reduce the fis-
cal deficit and reliance on foreign savings, the current Mexican recession
could turn into a prolonged crisis, cbaracterized by a deepening and widening
of poverty and increased risks of instability.
C.     STRUCTIRAL POLICY REFORMS
42.    Policy adjustments needed to recover sustainable growth over the medium
term include both trade liberalization and a number of other reforms. Many of
these were outlined in the Government's 1982 National Development Plan and
reaffirmed in the February 21 statement by President de la Madrid.  As noted
earlier, crucial trade policy reforms--to move Mexico from a now exhausted
inward-oriented growth path to sustainable outward-oriented growth--will create
opportunities for growth and employment based on Mexico's international compar-
ative advantages.   In the absence of such opportunities,  it will be difficult
or impossible to reduce subsidies or to carry out tiany of the other necessary
reforms.   Moreover,  the gradual reduction of trade policy distortions will
exert pressures for change in the whole structure of domestic production costs
and output prices, and will thus serve to identify other needed structural
adjustments and guide policy reforms accordingly, as the economy moves towards



- 21 -
international competitiveness and relative prices.   Trade reform, to be Buc-
cessful and lasting, must be accompanied by other reforms to enable the economy
to respond to the new opportunities that will emerge in a more open economy.
Hence, trade reform is at the heart of Mexico's structural adjustment needs,
and it is partly for that reason that the first quick-disbursing policy-based
Bank loan to Mexico is proposed in support of trade policy reform.
C.1    The Trade Liberalization Program:  1986-88
43.     Trade policy reform is the first area in which major structural reform
is being undertaken.  The trade policy program that would be supported by the
proposed loan is described in the Government's Statement of Foreign Trade Poli-
cy (Annex IV). The measures constituting this program and their implementation
schedule are discussed separately in Part IV below.
44.    Within the complementary context of the stabilization program described
in the previous section, the trade liberalization program is aimed at achieving
a gradual, orderly, and programmed opening of the Mexican economy through a
rationalization of the trade incentive regime, elimination of distortions and
controls in the foreign exchange markets, and a reduction of effective protec-
tion.  The trade liberalization program involves a change in the instrumenta-
tion of protection--from QRs and ORPs to tariffs--and a programmed reduction
over time in tariffs and their dispersion (Figure 32).  The net effect of this
program will be to permit Mexico to exploit its comparative advantages in manu-
facturing,  agriculture,  and mining,  as described  in Annex VII.   The result
should be to generate rapid growth of non-oil exports and efficient import
substitution, which are labor-intensive and hence employment-generating (see
Part III.D below).   This will occur in conjunction with exports that permit
economies of scale through increasing participation in the international
division of labor.

45.    In the appraisal and negotiation of the proposed loan, it became evident
that the effect on protection of QRs on imports and the use of ORPs for the
purpose of calculating import duties is very similar.  It was agreed that these
two protection devices could be aggregated on a net basis to measure the total
percentage of domestic production protected by NTBs.  As of March 31, 1986, it
was estimated that 55% of domestic production (Table VIII.13) was still covered
by QRs (compared to 100% at the start of 1985), and a further 17.5% by ORPs. A
number of import tariff categories are covered by both QRs and ORPs, such that
the net NTB coverage of domestic production as of March 31, 1986 was about
67.5%.   In loan negotiations,  it was agreed  that only  the net concept was
meaningful for the measurement of changes in protection afforded by NTBs.
46.      The proposed  trade reform program is expected to generate, over the
medium term, new exports to offset increased import demand, so that exports and
imports will rise together as a percentage of GDP as the economy moves towards
the more typical export-CDP ratios observed in other economies (Table 1).  In
the short term, the trade liberalization is not expected to have an unduly
negative impact on the balance of payments, because real economic activity is
likely to be depressed throughout 1986 (Figure 24) and the real exchange rate
is at an all-time record high level (Figure 4).
C.2    Other Structural Reforms
47.    In order to ensure that the economy can fully respond to and exploit new
opportunities for efficient growth and employment in a more open economy,
other structural adjustments will be needed.   The structural reforms outlined
below are those which, in the Bank's judgment, will be necessary over the
medium term. These reforms are broadly consistent with the Government's stated
intentions, and have in fact already begun to be implemented in a number of
areas.   The medium-term scenarios analyzed below assume that the Government
will seek ways to implement these adjustments expeditiously, particularly in
the following areas: public sector resource management; financial sector poli-
cy; industrial restructuring; agriculture; foreign investment and technology
transfer; and petroleum sector strategy.  The objectives of these reforms are
the resumption of growth in GDP and per capita consumption, together with a
gradual improvement in external creditworthiness.
48.    Public Sector Resource Management.  The overexpansion of the public sec-
tor after the early 1970s occurred in part to offset the lagging growth poten-
tial of an inward-oriented economy. As the economy is gradually opened, Mexico
will be able to commensurately scale back burdensome subsidies and inefficiency
in public sector resource mobilization and management. A number of reforms are
being considered and implemented  in this context:   Reform of the tax system;
reform of public sector pricing and subsidy policies; improvements in the
management or divestiture of parastatals; improvement in investment planning;
and reform of public sector procurement policies.
49.    Reliance on taxation of domestic activities (as distinct from oil export
revenue) will need to increase.   The Government is working on modification of
existing legislation and administrative practices, including actions to elimi-
nate bias against equity financing, close loopholes, and reduce tax evasion.



- 23 -
The goal is to reverse the downward trend of non-oil tax revenues in recent
years (Figure 5).  Public sector pricing of tradeable goods will need to place
greater reliance on international prices, or be decontrolled altogether, in
order to increase the efficiency of resource use and reduce implicit subsi-
dies.   The goal would be to continue the downward trend since the early 198Ds
in the level of transfers and subsidies (Figure 6), to decontrol prices, and to
increase the real level of controlled prices.   In addition,  the Government is
now implementing the divestiture of up to 200 state companies.  Public invest-
ment, which fell to below 7% of GDP in 1984-85 for the first time since the
early 1970s (Figure 18) and is expected to fall below 6% of GDP in 1986, would
need to gradually recover and be redirected in coming years into areas such as
basic infrastructure and human resourcesp both of which have been severely af-
fected by the austerity efforts since 1983.  There is a need for improved proj-
ect evaluation and selection, and more integrated public investment programming
over a multiyear horizon with a closer link to assessments of financing avail-
ability. Finally, public sector procurement policies would need to be refornmu-
lated so as to encourage a more competitive and internationally efficient
allocation of such purchases.
50.     Financial Sector Policy.  Mexico's financial system grew rapidly until
the early 1970s, but has suffered a prolonged cyclical decline since then
(Figure 9).  The decline accelerated after 1982, as noted in Part I, when the
Government nationalized the banking system and forcibly converted foreign-cur-
rency deposits.  Credit allocation and interest rate controls have been stead-
ily expanded to cover 75% of commercial bank assets.   Meanwhile, with rising
fiscal deficits over the same period, the public sector share in total credit
(Figure 11) has increased from less than 25% in the early 1970s to nearly 80%
by 1985.
51.    Increasing credit availability to the private sector will become a vital
element in the process of adjustment to a more open economy. Mexico will need
to reverse the severe erosion of its financial system, by gradually deregulat-
ing interest rates and credit allocation schemes.   Declining fiscal deficits
should then absorb declining shares of increased domestic financial savings and
make available sizeable increases in credit to the private sector.
52.     Exchange Rate System.   As noted in Parts I and II, the exchange rate
was devalued sharply in real terms in 1982.  A dual exchange rate was intro-
duced as a transitory measure and has been maintained since then.  Until mid-
1985, the gap between the controlled and the so-called "free" (also controlled)
rates declined steadily from more than 50% to about 7.5% (Figure 33). Together
with the trade liberalization measures of July 25, 1985, the authorities deval-
ued  the controlled  rate by 17% and  allowed  the "free rate" to float.   On
August 7, they abandoned the system of preannouncing the daily rate of depre-
ciation in the controlled market for one month ahead, and adopted instead a
managed float.   Since July,  the controlled rate has depreciated significantly
faster than domestic inflation, but the spread between the controlled and free
rates widened to nearly 50% by late 1985 (Figure 33).  So far in 1986 the con-
trolled rate has depreciated by about 40% in real terms between mid-1985 and
mid-1986 (Figure 4), and the spread has narrowed to less than 5% during the
first five months of 1986. As a result of the new managed float, the drain on
international reserves that occurred during the first five months of 1985 was



- 24 -
halted, and indeed some actual strengthening of Mexico's reserve position oc-
curred in the early months of 1986, which, however, has recently deteriorated
again.
Figure 33

53.    While the controlled exchange market covers about 80Z of trade and pay-
ments transactions, the existence of a premium on the parallel market rate is
an inducement to under- and overinvoicing, and is a discentive to non-oil
exports. The presence of exchange market controls has encouraged the develop-
ment of the parallel market, giving rise to distortions, capital flight, and
the risk of unfavorable effects on confidence.   The Government has recently
reiterated its commitment to exercise flexibility in the management of exchange
rate policy and to seek to minimize exchange rate differentials by adjusting
the controllcd rate as needed.   An early unification of rates would have a
positive impact on confidence and would further improve the environment for
exporters.
54.     Industrial Restructuring.  A large share of heavy industrial and basic
goods production is accounted for by parastatals such as PEMEX, FERTIMEX, and
SIDERMEX, some of which are internationally uncompetitive and would need to be
restructured in consonance with the trade liberalization program.  A plan for
the restructuring of Hexico's railroads, both physically and financially, was
adopted in 1985 (with the support of a Bank loan), and actions planned for 1986
include restructuring of parastatals in the areas of steel, fertilizers, ship-
building, and sugar.
55.   Policy measures which expose the Mexican economy to greater international
competition will place substantial competitive pressure on activities with a
relatively high domestic resource cost (DRC) ratio, such as rubber products,
plastics, basic and secondary iron and steel products, consumer electronics,
and automobiles, and will exert some competitive pressure on the activities
with uncertain international competitiveness, such as clothing, wood products,
basic  chemicals,  nonferrous metals,  and non-metallic minerals.   Successful



- 25 -
adjustment to a liberalization effort will therefore require productivity gains
(and hence reductions in unit costs of production) of sufficient magnitude to
achieve acceptable levels of international competitiveness.   Industries with
uncertain international competitiveness and those classified as uncompetitive
are concentrated in intermediate and capital and durable goods.  In general,
and as expected, high levels of effective protection are associated with
uncompetitive activities, for example, automobiles, electronic and electrical
apparatus, basic iron and steel, and structural steel products.   The low and
negative protection to most consumer goods industries is similarly reflected in
adequate levels of efficiency in such activities as food products, beverages,
and soaps and detergents.
56.     By international standards, Mexico's productivity performance was poor
during the 1970s.  The mean rate of total factor productivity CTFP) change is
similar to that encountered in other heavily protected economies, such as
Portugal, the Philippines, and India, and is well below the rates of such
trading competitors as the United States, Japan, Korea, Thailand, and Turkey.
Mexico has relatively high variability in the distribution of TFP growth rates
as well; the coefficient of variation of 3.2 is high for the level of per
capita income of the economy.
57.     Industrial sector adjustment to trade policy reform will require that
firms in adversely affected sectors improve their cost competitiveness via
accelerated productivity performance, if they are to adapt to the new price
regime.   Information on DRC levels, when combined with that on productivity
change, provides some insight into the magnitude of the structural adjustment
problem for Mexican industry.   Industries with acceptable levels of interna-
tional competitiveness, as measured by the DRC, will need to maintain satisfac-
tory levels of productivity change by international standards in order to
preserve or increase their market share.   Industrial sectors which are not
competitive  by  international  standards  will  require  rates  of productivity
change which exceed those of international competitors, if they are to adjust
successfully to the change in relative prices.
58.    The character of the structural adjustment problem can be seen from the
analysis contained in Annex VII. The only activities in which Mexico is both
internationally competitive and shows acceptable productivity performance are
the traditional light industries with  limited protection.   Auto bodies and
parts is the only industry with uncertain but improving international
competitiveness, which is consistent with its export performance; while rubber
products and structural steel are not presently competitive, but are improving
relative to potential external competitors.
59.    A wide range of currently competitive activities are apparently losing
ground to potential international competitors, including such important inter-
mediate goods producers as fertilizers. glass products, cement, chemicals, and
wood products.  Rising levels of inefficiency in these activities will affect
downstream users reducing levels of international competitiveness further.
Capital goods and consumer durables are heavily concentrated in grolps classi-
fied as of uncertain or unacceptable levels of international competitiveness.
60.    If the private sector is to respond to the emerging structure of incen-
tives of a more open economy, it is important tnat the Government continue



- 26 -
making every effort to improve the private sector investment climate.  This
requires stability and clarity of policy rules, adequate credit availability,
and a phaslng down of unnecessary interventions in pricing and internal and
external trade.
61.     Agricultural Sector Polic.  Agriculture hos been especially penalized
by declining or increasingly negative effective protection over the past quar-
ter century (see Part IV). As the domestic terms of trade were turned in favor
of industry, against agriculture and other primary sectors (including mining,
fishery, and forestry), Mexican agricultural growth rates declined ateadily
from 4.8% per year in the period 1930-60 to 3.4% in the 1960s, and 2% during
the 1970s. From being a net exporting sector, agriculture turned into a large
net importer, increasingly dependent on credit and input subsidies to offset
the anti-export bias and the negative effective protection emanating from the
trade regime.
62.      As the trade regime is liberalized,  and industrial protection dimin-
ished, agriculture is a major potential beneficiary.   But in order to enable
the sector to respond to these improvements in the incentive regime, Mexico
would need to gradually liberalize trade in agricultural goods and deregulate
the pervasive Government controls on the pricing, subsidization, and marketing
of agricultural  inputs and production.   Since these reforms are inseparably
linked, the Bank has offered to support the Government's trade liberalization
program in agriculture under a separate agricultural sector loan.   In parti-
cular, the role and activities of CONASUPO, the food marketing agency, is being
reexamined and scaled back.   Also, with renewed opportunities for productive
and profitable activities in agriculture, Mexico will benefit from the
announced completion of land reform by 1988.
63.     Foreign Iuve.tuent and Technology Transfer.  As Mexico moves toward a
more open trade regime, it will enhance the benefits of that openness, includ-
ing its external competitiveness, by also becoming more open to foreign invest-
ment and technology transfer.   This will be particularly the case if Mexico
wants to replace foreign credit with foreign equity participation.  Mexico is
seeking a substantial increase in net new foreign investment, over the average
of US$400 million during the period 1983-85, but this is likely to occur only
with significant modernization of the foreign investment laws. Export-oriented
foreign investment offers the best prospects over the medium term, attracted by
lower labor costs, which are now lower in Mexico than in the Far East, and a
liberalized trading environment.   The most effective adjustment might be to
publish clear and simple rules for certain broad classes of export-oriented
investment and technology transfers, such as are already embedded in the
"maquila" (in-bond) legislation, which has in recent years been considerably
extended and liberalized outside the border areas (see para. 9).  Recent deci-
sions to approve joint ventures and full-ownership foreign investments in
selected areas indicate the Government's shift to a more flexible policy.
64.     Petrolem Sector Strategy.  The emerging situation in the world petro-
leum markets will adversely affect Mexico in the short run, but also creates an
opportunity to fundamentally reassess its petroleum production and export



- 27 -
strategy in the light of similarly fundamental changes taking place in the
strategies of other major world producers.  Even at lower oil prices interna-
tionally, Mexico ia likely to continue deriving a large economic surplus and
other benefits from its oil resources.  Towards that end, Mexico would have to
maintain flexible export volume and pricing policies and maximize the contribu-
tion of oil revenues to the balance of payments and public finances.
65.       A  reassessment of medium-term  hydrocarbons  policy  should  reflect
Mexico's position as the holder of the fifth largest proven oil and gas re-
serves in the world (Annex VIII, Table 26), as well as Mexico's position among
low-cost  producers worldwide  (Annex VIII,  Table 34).   A medium-term  price
scenario of US$15 per barrel should lead to an increase in the rate of growth
of world demand and a decline in supplies from high-cost producing areas, whieh
may represent as much as 502 of total production from non-OPEC, non-Communist
areas.   In such an environment, Mexico could expect to increase its share of
rising world demand over the projection period. At present, Mexico holds 7.2%
of proven world oil and gas reserves, but, despite its cost advantages, ac-
counts for only 5.7% of total world production.  Thus, in terms of its produc-
tion-to-reserves ratio, Mexico is well below internationaL standards, though
its relative cost advantage suggests that this ratio could be higher.
66.    So far in 1986, total export volume has declined to an expected average
of 1.35 million barrels per day (mbd) in the first six months of 1986, thus
aggravating  the revenue loss from declining oil prices.   This has occurred
because Mexico has used lagged pricing formulae that tended to price Mexican
oil out of the markets in declining price situations. By February 1986, Mexico
made a strong bid to recover target export volumes through a major price reduc-
tion and its decision to introduce daily price fixing--linked to spot markets--
beginning in March. In April, Mexico adopted a netback formula to become fully
competitive, and export volume has increased from 1.1 mbds in early 1986 to
1.45 mbds expected for June.
67.     Because of the extreme stringency of resources experienced by Mexico at
the present time, the actual level of investments by PEMEX, Mexico's State oil
company, has fallen sharply since the late 1970s and early 1980s. Before this
fall in investment, it had been estimated that Mexico needed an annual rate of
investment of US$2-3 billion to maintain a production level of 3 mbds.  There
is, in fact, some concern that the current investment level is only barely suf-
ficient to maintain existing capacity. Since at US$15 per barrel or even some-
what lower prices the economic rate of return on oil investments in Mexico
remains very high--probably higher than in most sectors of the economy--it is
important for Mexico to explore ways in which the level of petroleum sector
investments can be increased.   It may, therefore, be worthwhile for Mexico to
consider risk-sharing arrangements in the exploration and drilling for new oil.
D.     SOCIAL IMPACT OF TRADE POLICY AND TRADE LIBERALIZATIOE
68.       Social development,  income distribution,  and  employment have  been
adversely affected by Mexico's import-subtituting industrialization strategy.
Agriculture, which was once a flourishing, labor-intensive, and net-exporting
sector, became a heavy net importer and slowed its absorption of labor. Growth



- 28 -
of income and employment in rural areas declined with the rise in the anti-
export bias of the trade regime and the associated resource pull toward indus-
try and capital intensive production for domestic markets.  These factors--and
the bureaucratization in Mexico City of discretionary trade and other incen-
tives--promoted urban and regional concentration on a scale almost unequalled
anywhere else.
69.     Detailed calculations of labor intensity in different sectors of the
Mexican economy have found that employment generation in exporting sectors on
the average exceeds that of the econonr  as a whole by 351-40% (Annex VIII,
Table 30). As noted in Part I, the inward-biased trade regime contributed to
rising unemployment--formal or unrecorded, very low wages for non-union non-
governmental workers,  and outmigration.    Statistical estimates suggest that
expatriate Mexican workers, as a percent of the total Mexican work force, rose
from 5.1% in 1940 to 22.6% by 1975 (Annex VIII, Table 33), and is likely to
have risen still further since then.
70.    The high levels of quantitative and other barriers to foreign competi-
tion over the period 1982-85, combined with a 30% decline in real wages, per-
mitted most firms to remain profitable and indeed to reduce their debt burdens
significantly (in real terms).  Despite the stressful economic environment of
this period, there has been little or no industrial bankruptcy. Similarly, the
large real wage decline cushioned the unemployment that would otherwise have
resulted from the 1982-84 recession.
71.    An orderly redirection of Mexico's incentive regime towards a more open-
economy growth path is expected to increase the labor intensity of new invest-
ments--given Mexico's internationally low-cost labor resources--and hence the
rate of growth of wages and employment in relation to overall economic growth.
Similarly, the bias of a trade regime that favored inefficient low-quality pro-
ducers over consumers will be lessened.  But in the short run, there will be
some firms that may be forced into bankruptcy, and, consequently, there will be
subsectors or industries that cannot expect to become internationally competi-
tive. Other firms with a potential for exports and efficient import substitu-
tion will be able to grow and increase their employment, even despite an
overall stagnation of the economy in the short run.  It is interesting to note
that the in-bond ("maquila") sector has grown rapidly in both production and
employment over the past several years (as noted in para. 9).   Finally,  the
heavy manpower resources that have been absorbed by administration of the QR
system can be released Eor productive activities that will emerge as the
economy opens up.
E.     THE ETERRAL nIAUCIUG PfOGRA  POR 1986-88
72.    Just as the macro policy framework must be supportive of trade liberali-
zation, so the external financing needs of Mexico must be met if these reforms
are to be successful. The Bank alone cannot provide more than a modest pro-
portion of Mexico's projected external financing needs for 1986-88. Therefore,
the Bank has emphasized to the Government that the proposed TPL cannot by it-
self be viewed as sufficient to ensure the viability of the trade liberaliza-
tion program. Rather, it should be viewed as a catalyst for other forms of
lending that may be mobilized in parallel with the TPL.



- 29 -
73.     The lack of net new external financing so far in 1986 has exacerbated
Mexico's macroeconomic difficulties.   It is therefore a key objective of the
proposed TPL to provide a stimulus to other external lenders, particularly the
commercial banks, to join in a concerted international effort to mobilize suit-
able external financing for Mexico over the period 1986-88 in support of an
appropriate program of stabilization and growth-oriented adjustment. Presenta-
tion of this loan to the Board is being made before the full external financing
package has been assembled for Mexico, so as to facilitate the catalytic role
of the proposed loan through its recognition of, and support for, Mexico's
trade liberalization program.   However,  the loan as proposed will not become
effective until the Bank receives satisfactory assurances that Mexico's
external financing needs are likely to be met.
F.     TEE ROLE OF TEE BANK
74.     In the past, the Bank has supported Mexico's development efforts with
investment and credit projects in a wide range of sectors, including agricul-
ture, rural development, infrastructure, housing, industrial finance, etc.
Following the crisis of 1982, the Bank shifted the primary focus of its activi-
ty from projects to a greater concentration on the policy dialogue and lending
to support structural change.  The Bank has carried out an intensified policy
dialogue with the Government in the wake of the 1982 crisis in the context of
its economic and sector work, and has sponsored or undertaken studies in a
number of areas, including industrial and trade policy, export promotion,
agriculture policy, interest rate and credit subsidy policy, and public sector
investment. The policy dialogue in the area of export development policy has
been supported by the first Export Development Project of 1983. As part of the
Bank's preparation of the present loan proposal, and other possible loans that
would support Mexican policy reforms, discussions have been carried on for some
time concerning major policy reforms in the areas of public sector investment,
parastatal management, and housing.  The policy dialogue on interest rate and
credit subsidies resulted in the General Interest Rate Agreement (GIRA), and
continues as ;.art of the implementation of that Agreement.
75.     As noted in Section C, the first area in which the Government proposes
to undertake adjustments under its 1986-88 structural reform program, to be
implemented in parallel with a continuation of the stabilization program, is to
make the economy more competitive through the reduction of trade barriers, the
maintenance of an attractive real exchange rate, and further expansion of the
free-trade environment for exporters.   The Government's  trade liberalization
program, together with an appropriate exchange rate and macro policy framework,
is to be supported by the proposed loan.  In addition, other areas of policy
adjustment are under consideration.   The further expansion of the free-trade
environment for exporters is to be supported by a Second Export Development
Project loan, which is in an advanced stage of preparation.  The Government's
program also includes rationalization and restructuring of public sector enter-
prises and public sector investment, to be supported by a Technical Assistance
loan whirh is currently under preparation; reduction of financial subsidies, as
provided under GIRA; and agricultural sector adjustments to facilitate the
shift to a more open economy.   The Bank is engaged in a policy dialogue with
the authorities on the possibility of financial and technical support from the
Bank for an agricultural adjustment program that will enable the sector to
become an active beneficiary of the shift to a more open economy.



- 30 -
76.    Bank policy-based lending operations, such as the proposed Trade Policy
Loan, would provide vital financial support during this difficult period for
Mexico.  The Bank would assume a considerable share of total financing during
the 1986-88 period, to support the structural changes necessary for Mexico to
move onto a path of high and sustainable growth and employment and to restore
Mexico's longer-temn financial stability and creditworthiness. The Bank's pro-
gram would provide a vehicle for attracting additional financing from other
official and private commercial sources that are interested in becoming asso-
ciated with Bsnk-supported reforms.
C.     THE ROLE OF THE [IW
77.    The IMF has supported two major stabilization programs in Mexico since
1976.  An Extended Fund Facility was negotiated in 1976, but no further draw-
ings under the program took place after early 1977.   In 1978, the Government
repurchased its early drawings. A second EFF was negotiated in late 1982 for a
total of SDRs 3.6 billion, covering the three-year period through December
1985.  Because of noncompliance with some of the quantitative performance cri-
teria for end-June and end-September 1985, Mexico did not undertake the draw-
ings scheduled for August and November.  A total of SDRs 908 million remained
undrawn under that facility.  In early 1986, the IMF approved a special emer-
gency assistance drawing in support oE Mexico's earthquake reconstruction
needs, in the amount of SDRs 291 million.  It was understood in connection with
that drawing that Mexico would come to an agreement with the IKF on a new
program for 1986.
78.    There have been detailed dipcussions with Fund staff on the program of
measures and the objectives of the proposed loan.
B.     CREDrT ORTRINESS
79.    At the end of 1985, the Bank's share in Mexico's public debt was 5.1%.
The Bank's share in Mexico's total public external interest payments was 4.3%.
The Bank's exposure in Mexico as of end-1985 amounted to 7.6% of its total. Ir.
view of the expectation that sound economic policies will be pursued in the
future, Mexico is considered creditworthy for this loan.
PART IV:   THE PROPOSED LOAM
A.     LOAN HISTORY
80.    The Bank and the Government have engaged, since late 1984, in an active
dialogue on ways to implement the Government's objective announced in the 1982
National Development Plan of gradually opening the Mexican economy.   In 1983,
the Bank approved the first Export Development loan, in support of a liberal-
ized import regime for exporters. Beginning in mid-1984, the Bank indicated to
the Government that it would be willing to consider support for an action pro-
gram of general import and trade policy reform through additional lending in
the form of one or more fast-disbursing loans.  The Trade Policy Loan program
has been discussed with the Government as a multiyear framework for Bank finan-
cial and technical support, and as a basis on which to mobilize additional
financing from commercial sources.

first Trade Policy Loan in Septombor, and completod appraisal on November 25,
1985, after an interruption due to the September 19 earthquako.  Negotiations
