B.     LOAN OBJECTIVES AND THil TRADE LIBERALIZATION PROGRAM
82.    The proposed US$500 million Trade Policy Loan (TPL) is intended to sup-
port implementation of Mexico's program to liberalise its trade regime and open
the economy to achieve greater domestic efficiency and international competi-
tiveness, as the means to generate growth and employment through non-oil export
diversification  and reduced dependence on subsidies.   The proposed  loan  is
conceived as the first in a series of possible repeater operations over the
three-year period (1986-8B) covered by the trade policy reform program of the
Government. Given the far-reaching changes that will occur virtually through-
out the economy and the need to implement reforms in an orderly and gradual
fashion to allow time for the needed adjustments at the firm and sectoral lev-
els, it is clear that trade liberalization will need to be phased over a period
of several years and may require continued Bank assistance beyond 1988.   The
program is based on the recognition that this objective can best be achieved by
a gradual, preannounced, and programmed reduction of tariffs and the elimina-
tion as far as possible of remaining discretionary barriers to trade, such as
QRs and ORPs.  Both are referred to as non-tariff barriers (NTBs).  The pro-
posed loan may also assist the Government in mobilizing complementary funding
from commercial sources.
83.     The Bank estimates that, on the basis of the Government's prevent plans
(Part III.C) for the phasing down of NTBs, it is likely that the production-
weighted coverage of NTBs will decline from 1002 at end-1984--and 67.51 as of
March 31, 1986--to between 30 and 402 by end-October 1988.  The present struc-
ture of NTB coverage, as reflected in Table 3, will likely show a virtual elim-
ination of NTBs for the manufacturing sector, a partial elimination for agri-
culture, and little or no elimination for the oil sector.  Assuming that NTBs
will continue to be in effect (by October 1988) for basic grains and some other
subsectors in agricultural sector programs; for automobiles, pharmaceuticals,
and computers (these are not scheduled to expire until after 1988); for oil and
derivatives; and for products hazardous to public health and safety, it can be
estimated that about a third of production would then remain under NTBs.  How-
ever,  it should be noted that:  oil and derivatives alone account for 21% of
production (Table 3), where NTBs have little or no protective effect since
Mexico is an oil exporter; the sector programs in manufacturing that would
remain under NTBs account for less than 3%; and most of agriculture (apart from
basic grains) is internationally competitive. Hence, the expected fTB coverage
by end-1988 will have only limited protection effects, and would not materially
affect the credibility or effectiveness of the announced tariff calendar.

84.     The Government is cognizant of the need to have a preannounced QR/NTB
reduction calendar.  Such a calendar was indeed announced in October 1984, for
a period  extending through to 1989.1/   This calendar was superseded by the
drastic QR reduction on July 25, 1985.  Following the measures of July 25, the
Government announced its intention to prepare a new revised calendar for
publication; but this plan was soon overtaken by Mexico's decision to seek
accession to GATT. Once the GATT negotiations were begun in November 1985, the
Government felt it would be unwise to publicly commit further QR reductions,
lest Mexico's negotiating position be undermined in advance.   The Government
informed  the Bank  that  no QR  or NTB  calendars could  be committed  before
completion  of  the  GATT  negotiations,  now  expected  by  July  1986.    The
Government'c  broad  intentions  in  this  regard  (see para.  95  (iii-iv))  are
included in Annex IV, Mexico's Statement of Foreign Trade Policy.

86.      The Government's trade policy reform program will be published  in
Mexico, including  interim adjustments to the end-1988 targets.   The Govern-
ment's Statement of Foreign Trade Policy (Annex IV) will be published in Mexico
prior to Board presentation, including the tariff reduction calendar through

(i)    Reduction of the maximum tariff from 100% to 45%;
(ii)    Reduction of the average tariff to 24.1%;
(iii)    Reduction of the number of tariffs to a maximum of 8; and
(iv)    Reduction of NTBs to a maximum of 67.5% of production.
C.     THE BORROWER
87.     The National Bank for Foreign Trade (BANCOMEXT) will be the Borrower,
and will be responsible, with the support of the Secretariat of Finance (SHCP),
the Secretariat of Trade and Industrial Promotion (SECOFI), and the Central
Bank (Banco de Mexico),  for administering the proposed loan.   BANCOMEXT has
recently been granted a new charter that greatly expands its policy, financing,



- 35 -
and technical roles in all aspects of Mexico's foreign trade.   Hence,  it can
effectively act as both borrower for the proposed loan, and focal point for the
monitoring of, and technical support for, the trade reform process.
D.     DISBURSEMENT
88.      The proposed  loan would be disbursed  in two  tranches:    thle  first
tranche, of US$300 million would become available upon effectiveness, and the
second tranche, of US$189 million, would become available upon fulfillment of
conditions for second tranche release, but not later than December 31, 1986.
If the latter conditions are not met by that date, the second tranche will be
cancelled, unless otherwise agreed between the Bankc and Mexico. The balance of
US$11 miLlion, for the financing of collateral studies and the establishment of
a modern antidumping system by Mexico, would be disbursed more slowly, but
before November 30, 1988, which is the closing date for the proposed loan.
89.       Conditions  of  effectiveness (first  tranche  disbursement)  are  that:
(i) the Bank shall have been furnished with evidence, satisfactory to the Bank,
that the Government's macropolicy framework is consistent with the trade liber-
alization objectives of the Trade Policy Program (hereafter referred to as the
Program) being supported by this Loan; and (ii) the Bank shall have received
satisfactory assurances that the Government's external financing needs for 1986
will be me..  In addition, the Bank will, prior to the date of effectiveness,
monitor progress in the execution of the Program, in particular with regard to
the maintenance of the policies outlined in para. 96.
90.    Conditions for second-tranche release.  Release of the second tranche of
US$189 million of the Loan would be contingent upon:
(i)    Satisfactory progress with respect to continued implementation of
the Program;
(ii)    Macroeconomic policy framework of the Government  is consistent
with the Program;
(iii)    Reduction, no later than December 31, 1986, of the coverage of
Non-Tariff Barriers by not less than 5% of total production of
goods in Mexico, vis-a-vis the coverage existing on March 31,
1986; and
(iv)   Publication of internationally acceptable regulations for estab-
lishment of an administrative antidumping system.
E.     PROCUREMENf AND ADMINISTRATION
91.     Proceeds of the loan would be used to reimburse 100% of the foreign
exchange cost of eligible imports and collateral studies. Eligible imports for
purposes of this loan shall be defined broadly as those imports procured after
January 1, 1986 that fall into categories ("fracciones") that were freed from
QRs on or after July 25, 1985.   Ineligible imports include goods subject to
ORPs or QRs of any kind; imports financed from other official multilateral or
bilateral sources; and goods intended for military or paramilitary use. A list
of ineligible imports is included in the Annex to Schedule I of the Loan
Agreement.



- 36 -
92.     BANCOMEXT will be responsible for maintaining loan accounts and for the
preparation and submission of withdrawal applications.  Disbursements from the
proposed loan would be made on the basis oE a summary from the Banco de Mexico
detailing individual transactions in each relevant period in respect of eligi-
ble imports, together with a certification from the Banco de Mexico of payment
of the amounts involved and of their eligibility under the loan. Applications
for withdrawals will be consolidated and submitted in amounts not less than
US$1 million.
93.    Both private and public sector imports would be eligible for financing.
Contracts under US$5 million each would be awarded on the basis of the normal
procurement practices of the purchaser.  Contracts for other goods and servi-
ces, estimated to cost US$5 million or more each, will be procured through
international competitive bidding in accordance with Bank Guidelines.
F.     MONITORING, REPORTING, AND AUDITING
94.     The proposed Trade Policy Loan (TP) and Mexico's trade liberalization
program will require monitoring of trade policy targets stipulated in the
Government's action calendar attached to the Statement of Foreign Trade Policy
(Annex IV), as well as regular assessments of the adequacy of the macro policy
framework.   Possible proposals for follow-up TPLs will,  in part, be based on
progress towards the Government's objectives.
95.    The Borrower (BANCOMEXT) will prepare a quarterly report on progress and
trends in foreign trade and trade policy, including a quarterly assessment of
eEfective protection levels. The Bank will monitor the progress of implementa-
tion of the program through regular supervision and exchange of views with the
Borrower and the Government, covering both policy instrument targets and per-
formance targets as described above and in particular, but not limited to: the
phasing out of QRs and ORPs; the reduction of tariff dispersion; the reduction
of effective protection; the growth of non-oil exports; and the maintenance of
an adequate real effective exchange rate.  The Bank would rely on the IMP for
the monitoring of key macroeconomic policies and indicators.
G.     COLLATERAL STUDIES AND TECHNICAL ASSISTANCE
96.    A key element of the Bank's proposed contribution to the success of the
trade liberalization program consists of a program of collateral studies and
technical assistance to provide timely and in-depth analysis of and support for
the implementation of the three-year trade reform program.   The proposed TPL
includes for this purpose a technical assistance component in the amount of
US$11 million, divided in two parts. The amount of US$3 million will be avail-
able to finance a series of studies, which may include major studies on primary
areas of policy reform needed to complement trade liberalization, e.g., indus-
trial restructuring and sectoral programming in support of improved interna-
tional  competitiveness;  agricultural  sector  adjustment;  financial  sector
adjustment; and procurement and other trade-related policies of the public
sector.   The second part,  in the amount of US$8 million, will be available to
finance support for the establishment of a modern antidumping system and a
system of full quarterly computerized updating of effective protection
calculations.



- 37 -
B.     PROJECT BENEFITS
97.    The main justification for the proposed Bank loan is that it will pro-
vide direct financial and technical support to the Government in a particularly
critical phase of the medium-term program of trade poLicy reform and economic
restructuring.   The expected benefit of the program is to induce a long-term
reorientation of the Mexican economy, away from inefficient import substitution
behind high protective barriers, towards greater international competitiveness
in both non-oil exports and the production for domestic markets, as discussed
in Annex VI.  The phasing out of quantitative import restrictions and ORPs is
also expected to reduce inflationary pressures in the economy, improve the ef-
fectiveness of overall demand-management policies of the Government, and reduce
the vulnerability of the economy to wide swings in response to small deviations
in policy, as discussed in Part III.
98.     The second major justification for the proposed TPL program is that it
will provide a basis on which the Government can mobilize needed additional
financing from commercial sources.  At the time of the negotiations in 1983-84
that led to the restructuring of Mexico's external debt (as noted in Part [It),
the shared expectation of the Government and the international financial commu-
nity was that Mexico would successfully complete its stabilization program with
the support of the Extended Fund Facility of the IMF, and thus recover suffi-
cient external confidence and creditworthiness to return to voluntary lending
by 1986.   These expectations have not been borne out, and the new financing
needed for 1986 will be difficult for Mexico to obtain under the best of cir-
cumstances.  Commercial lenders have since late 1985 indicated to the Govern-
ment and the Bank that major new lending in 1986 would not be possible without
a new IMF program and structural reforms supported by the Bank, particularly in
the area of trade liberalization. The Bank has indicated to commercial lenders
that parallel arrangements would be possible on the basis of the proposed Trade
Policy Loan and the closely related EDP-II loan, which is expected to be ready
for presentation to the Executive Directors in a few months.
99.    It will be key to the success of the trade liberalization that external
financing be assured on a realistic and timely basis.  Net new financing needs
for 1986 were estimated by the Bank (at the time of appraisal) at US$3.0 bil-
lion at an assumed oil price of US$20 per barrel (as discussed in Part III).
Under an assumed oil price of US$12 per barrel, the total net new financing
need is currently estimated by the Government to be US$5.3 billion, r-f which
some US$2.5 billion is expected from commercial bank sources (see Annex IV).
100.   The proposed TPL would support the original objective of the Government
of undertaking the structural reforms needed to achieve sustainable growth and
employment and to recover external creditworthiness and confidence sufficient
to return to normal commercial lending over the medium term.  It is thus not
the purpose of the proposed loan to displace the financial markets, but rather
to facilitate Mexico's return to commercial lending.  Therefore, the parallel
cofinancing arrangements that are envisioned would entail the comfort of direct
Bank support to Mexico and Bank monitoring of the proposed trade liberalization
program.



- 38 -
I.     PROJECT RISKS
101.    The principal risk of the trade reform program is the possibility that
the macropolicy framework may become inconsistent with a gradual opening of the
economy.  Trade liberalization attempts have failed in the past in Mexico, as
elsewhere, not because of tariff and QR reduction, but because of overly expan-
sive macro policy or an overvalued exchange rate.
102.    Other risks of the trade reform program relate to the uncertainties in
the external environment, particularly if these are not offset on a timely and
appropriate  fashion by the macro-policy  framework.    External  risks  include
weakened export prospects and the possibility of further weakening of interna-
tional oil prices.  An important additional risk is failure to generate strong
expectations and adjustment with respect to the target tariff structure.  The
latter could occur for a variety of reasons, most prominently, a delay in
substantially reducing the level of NTB coverage from the present level of
67.5% of production.
103.   In addition, the proposed operation faces a risk that Mexico may be una-
ble to mobilize adequate and timely external financing.  The medium-term pros-
pects outlined in this report are based on an assumed average international
price of oil of US$13 per barrel and the expectation of substantial non-oil
export growth, achieved through active exchange rate policy, and fiscal and
monetary discipline, in addition to the trade liberalization.  Adverse external
circumstances, such as protectionism, a collapse in oil prices, or erosion of
domestic fiscal and monetary discipline, could affect the political commitment
to trade liberalization and make more difficult the attainment of adequate
rigor and credibility in the implementation of the trade reform program over a
sufficiently long period.   In the event of a marked deterioration in export
performance, due for e-aample to a collapse in oil prices, additional adjustment
efforts by Mexico (including an acceleration of the trade policy reform pro-
gram) will be required, and additional external financing needs may develop.
104.   There are, however, a number of significant elements in the Government's
program, and in the design of this loan, which reduce the above risks.  First,
the Government has demonstrated a strong commitment to trade policy reform by
the major steps taken during 1985 (Part IV), particularly the Government's
politically difficult decisions to drastically reduce QRs and to join GATT.
The Government has weighed the risks of a more open-economy development strate-
gy, and has come to the conclusion, as expressed in the Statement of Develop-
ment Policy, that Mexico has no viable alternative other than such an opening
for moving the economy onto a sustainable pattern of growth and high employ-
ment.
105.    Second,  the Government has taken drastic policy measures during late
1985 and early 1986 to recover the momentum of the successful stabilization
program of 1983-84.  A revised fiscal budget and policy program for 1986 are
currently being  formulated  (see Part  III.B).   These,  together with the TPL
program, represent key elements of a coherent strategy for recovering sound
macro policy and restructuring the economy.



- 39 -
106.    Measures are being, and will continue to be, implemented to facilitate
adjustment to a more open economy environment.   For example,  the new exchange
rate policy, consisting of a managed float, is designed to reflect inflation
trends and maintain adequate reserves, thus facilitating the use of exchange
rate adjustments in place of quantitative import controls to regulate the
balance of payments.
107.   Third, the trade policy program to be supported by the proposed TPL will
itself lend credibility and strength to the structural reform process, as dis-
cussed in Part III.  By announcing explicit, objective, and quantifiable tar-
gets for trade policy and a calendar of adjustments over three years to the
stipulated end-targets, the Covernment can achieve a sustained movement toward
the end-targets that is both gradual and credible.   Each calendar adjustment,
by itself, entails manageable political and economic costs. With the implemen-
tation of each successive calendar adjustment, the Government will be able to
build credibility for the end-targets, and thereby induce an orderly adjustment
by  firms  to the announced  target  incentive  regime.   The  transparency  of a
tariff-based trade policy, replacing the longstanding QR system, is critical to
this strategy.   Indeed,  the policy reforms of 1985, particularly the unprece-
dented reduction of QRs on July 25, have already created a widely shared recog-
nition that the Government is serious in its commitment to trade liberaliza-
tion.   Moreover,  the significant  financial support  that would  flow from the
Trade Policy Loan program would itself increase the domestic credibility of the
Covernment's trade liberalization program, in part by strengthening the balance
of payments and, thus, the perceived viability of trade liberalization.
PART V - BANK GROUP OPERATIONS IN MEXICO
A.     BANK OPERATIONS
108.    As of March 31,  1986,  Mexico had  received 95 loans  from the Bank,
amounting to US$8.29 billion, net of cancellations and terminations; of these,
61 loans totalling US$4.0 billion were fully disbursed.  The Bank held US$6.63
billion, of which US$2.68 billion had not yet been disbursed. Some 40% of Bank
lending has been for agriculture and rural development, 20% for industry, 9%
for power, and 18% for transportation; the remaining 13% has been for water
supply, tourism, urban development, and vocational training. Annex II contains
a summary statement of Bank loans as of March 31, 1986.
109.     Of  the US$8.29 billion  total  lending,  about US$4.7 billion was for
establishing or strengthening institutions for channelling credit to areas
where credit supply was deficient or non-existent, and setting up in the com-
mercial banking system the ability to carry out project-related appraisal of
investments in agriculture, industry, and tourism.  These credit programs have
facilitated lending to low-income farmers and small- and medium-scale indus-
trial and tourism enterprises.
110.    The Government arranged adequate budget financing in the years 1978 to
1981, which significantly improved project implementation. Government and Bank
officials met periodically to review project implementation, and greater atten-
tion was focussed on project monitoring.  As a result of these measures, most



- 40 -
of the Bank-assisted projects were being implemented satisfactorily until mid-
1982, and disbursements rose from US$91 million in FY78 to US$448 million in
FY82.  However, the 1982 financial crisis again caused delays in the provision
of counterpart funds; consequently, disbursements in FY83 declined to US$389
million.  A Special Action Program (SAP) was established in early 1983 to help
the Government by alleviating the counterpart funding constraints on develop-
ment projects, and 18 Bank-financed projects are receiving support under the
Program.   Partly as a result of the SAP,  disbursenents during FY84 improved
significantly at US$528.87 million,  or 35% over disbursements  in 1983.   The
FY85 disbursement figure is US$787.93 million, 49% over 1984 or 100% over
projected.
B.     IFC OPERATIONS
111.   As of March 31, 1986, IFC had made investment commitments in 27 compa-
nies in Mexico, for a total of US$753.9 million, of which US$580.2 million had
been sold, repaid, or cancelled.   A summary statement of IFC investments  is
presented  in Annex II.   IFC has been working  together with  the Bank  to:
Wi) identify investment opportunities which would best suit the needs of the
Mexican private sector in the current phase of the stabilization program, and
vii) assist the Government in reviewing the requirements of the country's capi-
tal markets, both through economic sector work and specific proposals for ven-
ture capital operations. A US$100 million capital goods facility, approved in
1983, has yet to be used, and is, therefore, under review.  Subsequently, the
XFC has carried out three operations in the motor vehicle and cotton seed
business.   The provision of foreign exchange and export production have been
the principal objectives of these operations.
C.     DAIS STRATEGY
112.    The Bank's major objectives in Mexico prior to the 1982 crisis were to:
Ca) support policies and programs leading to a wider distribution of the bene-
fits of economic growth; (b) help finance projects that, directly or indirect-
ly, contributed significantly to output and employment; (c) help reduce
Mexico's urban/regional imbalances; and (d) help free bottlenecks which prevent
rapid growth.  These continue to be important objectives of Bank assistance to
Mexico. However, following the 1982 economic crisis and a reassessment of the
Bank's role in Mexico, the Bank Management concluded that:  Ca) increased Bank
lending, critical to Mexico's recovery, must be linked to central policy re-
forms; and (b) the Bank should play a central role in assisting Mexico's return
to voluntary lending. To prepare itsel. for this new role, the Bank has inten-
sified and broadened its economic and sector work.  Specific policy reforms
that are presently being pursued through a dialogue with the Mexican Government
cover priority macroeconomic and cross-sectoral issues, such as trade liberali-
zation, rationalization of preferential credit systems, improvement in public
sector  pricing,  and  investment  and  subsidy reduction.   Traditional  lending
incorporating project- or sector-specific policy issues will be conducted in
parallel with policy dialogue on cross-sectoral or macro policy issues.
113.      Because of the difficult structural problems of agriculture and the
sector's crucial importance for the one-third of the nation's population living
in rural areas, the Bank has made agriculture the leading sector for its



- 41 -
lending.   The Bank's agricultural  lending program in Mexico aims at:   (a) a
more efficient and rational use of natural resources to increase production;
(b) productivity improvements of cultivated lands, with emphasis on the
productivity of small farmers; and (c) promotion of employment-generating
investments in rural areas. To support these goals, infrastructure investments
in Bank-assisted projects have been complemented with support services, such as
extension, marketing programs,  and credit.   The Bank has made 10  loans  in
FYs8O-85 totalling US$1,566.0 million, for irrigation, rural and agricultural
investment projects, and agro-industrial and livestock credit programs. A loan
for a Second Tropical Agricultural Project was approved by the Executive
Directors on March 4, 1986.  Projects for irrigation rehabilitation, extension
and research, seed multiplication, forestry, agro-industries, and agricultural
credit are in various stages of preparation.  Special emphasis has been placed
in recent years on the development of rainfed areas.
114.     Bank lending for industry between FYs8O-85 amounted to US$947.3 mil-
lion, covering areas of small- and medium-scsae industry, mining, vocational
training, capital goods industries development, and export development. After
the 1982 crisis, a US$350 million loan was approved to assist in Mexico's non-
oil export drive. Based on the lessons of experience under that sector adjust-
ment operation and broad economic sector work carried out with the Government
recently, a new comprehensive assistance strategy was formulated to guide the
Bank's lending work for industry.   This strategy supports:   (a) trade policy
reform to move towards greater uniformity of incentives and greater interna-
tional competitiveness; (b) complementary financial sector policies to reverse
the prolonged contraction experienced by Mexico's financial system, including
its securities market; and (c) measures required to encourage adequate supply
responses, particularly through export promotion and industrial restructuring.
115.    Under the above strategy, the Bank is responding to the current needs
of Mexico's industrial sector.   A US$500 million Trade Policy Loan, that has
just been negotiated and would be presented for Board consideration together
with the proposed project, is aimed at supporting the trade liberalization pro-
cess through further reform measures and the provision of quick-disbursing
finance that is expected to facilitate the mobilization of commercial bank
funds for Mexico's external financing needs in 1986.   A US$48 million Indus-
trial Technology Development Loan is also scheduled for distribution to the
Board, which would help support technological innovation at the enterprise lev-
el and iLmprove technology services.   The Bank has already appraised a Second
Export Development Project, which would continue Bank assistance for increased
non-oil exports and policy reforms and provide finance to exporters.  In addi-
tion, substantive preparation work has been undertaken to assess the need for
financial and physical restruccuring in key industrial sectors and explore the
ways in which the Bank could support both parastatal and private enterprises.
Discussions are most advanced for Fertilizer Sector Adjustment and Public
Sector Management Technical Assistance operations.
116.      Bank  lending  for transport  has  focussed  on regional development,
strengthening of institutions, and rationalization of public investment outlays
and pricing policies.  Between FYs8O-85, six loans amounting to US$753.7 mil-
lion were approved, including two in each of the following three subsectors:
highways, railways, and ports. Additional projects to support the above goals



- 42 -
were undertaken in the urban sector. During FYs8O-85, four loans were approved
in the urban sector totalling US$408.5 million, in the fields of water supply
and sewerage and urban development.  Tvo urban projects were recently approved
by the Executive Directors: a Municipal Strengthening and a Solid Waste Pilot
Project.  An Earthqua'-. Rehabilitation Project was also approved on March 25,
1986.  Additional projects are under consideration in the transport and urban
sectors, aiming at strengthening the various institutions in the areas of
planning, management, and finance.
117.     The Economic Development Institute (EDI) is assisting Mexico through
various courses/seminars dealing with policy alternatives and institutional
reforms.   EDI training is specifically directed at courses/seminars on water
supply and sanitation sector management, transport policy, agricultural policy,
industrial development and finance, and macro policy analysis.
118.    The Inter-American Development Bank (IDB) is the second largest source
of multilateral aid to Mexico.   The IDB has made loans to Mexico totalling
US$3.4 billion as of March 31, 1986.  Over 50% of the total has gone to agri-
cultural and rural development projects, and the balance to transportation,
industry, water supply and sewerage, tourism infrastructure, education, muni-
cipal development,  and preinvestment.   The IDB and the Bank have coordinated
their assistance on several projects.   Each has made loans  for the national
integrated rural development program (PIDER), agricultural and livestock cred-
it, small- and medium-scale industries development, and hotel development proj-
ects.   The  IDB  is also  providing  a program of assistance  for earthquake
rehabilitation and reconstruction.   The International Fund  for Agricultural
Development (IFAD) has approved a loan of US$22.0 million for a rural develop-
ment project in the State of Oaxaca, which was appraised by the Bank's staff
and for which the Bank is acting as cooperating institution for administering
the loan.
PART VI - RECOEElID&TION
119.    I am satisfied that the proposed loan would comply with the Articles of
agreement of the Bank, and recommend that the Executive Directors approve the
proposed loan.
Barber B. Conable
President
Attachments
July 14, 1986

AUU III
MMIECO: TRADE POLICY LOAN
Supplementary Project Data Sheet
Section 1: Timetable of Key Events
(a)  lime taken to Prepare Project:                  9 months
(b)  Project Prepared by:                            SHCP/BANCOMEXT/SECOFI
(c)  First Presentation to the Bank:                 August 1985
(d)  Departure of Appraisal Mission:                 September/November 1985
(e)  Completion of Negotiations:                     June 1986
(f)  Planned Date of Effectiveness:                  August/September 1986
Section II: Special Bank Implementation Actions
None
.Section III: Special Conditions
a)       The following are additional conditions of loan effectiveness:
I)    The Bank shall have been furnished evidence, satisfactory to the
Bank, that the Government's macro-economic policy framework is
consistent with the Trade Policy Program (hereinafter referred
to as the Program) being supported by this loan; and
ii)   The Bank has received satisfactory assurances that the Govern-
aent's external financing needs for 1986 will be met.
b)       Release of the second tranche of US$189 million of the loan would be
contingent upon:
i)    Satisfactory progress with respect to continued implementation
of the Program;
ii)   Macroeconomic policy framework of the Government is consistent
with the Program;
iii)  Reduction, no later than December 31, 1986, of the coverage of
Non-Tariff Barriers by not less than 5Z of total production of
goods in Mexico, vis-a-vis the coverage existing on March 31,
1986; and
iv)   Publication of internationally acceptable regulations for estab-
lishment of an administrative antidumping system.



- 52 -
AMEKX IV
Page 1 of 18
ME=ICO
COVEIEIT'S STATEMENT OF FOREIGN TRADE POLICY
Three-and-a-half years ago the Government of Mexico faced a daunt-
ing challenge.   Highly expansive domestic fiscal policies,  in a context of
abundant international credit, had led to the buildup of enormous internal
and external disequilibria.   By  late 1982,  the country's  foreign exchange
reserves were almost totally depleted, and inflation soared to unprecedented
levels.
Accordingly, from its very first day in offic:e, the Government of
President De la Madrid undertook the task of overcoming, the crisis and build-
ing new and healthier foundations  for future economic development.   It was
postulated from the beginning of his Administration, that the achievement of
these goals is mainly the responsibility of the Mexican people and their
Government.   However,  it is also clear  that an important role has to be
played, and should continue to be played, by the country's foreign creditors.
The 1983-85 Stabilizatin Drive
During the first years of the present Administration, perhaps of
necessity,  the emphasis was mainly on stabilization.   In this domain,  the
Government's efforts went well during 1983 and 1984: a sharp fiscal adjust-
ment brought dowa. the deficit from 18X of GDP to 9%; inflation came down; the
current account was in surplus by US$4 billion per annum; and growth began to
pick up.
On the external debt side, several rescheduling exercises and fresh
money arrangements for Mexico were successfully undertaken since the eruption
of the crisis in August 1982.  These constitute an excellent example of what
an open and friendly dialogue between a debtor country and its creditors can
achieve, as long as proper adjustment policies are practiced by the debtor
and a propitious international environment prevails.
Recently, however, the notion has circulated that Mexico has aban-
doned, or at least significantly relaxed its stabilization efforts, even
among those who not long ago held a very sympathetic view towards the coun-
try's adjustment policies.   In  the interuational perception,  the Mexican
economy has seemed to go from "miracle" to "mirage."
This perception has stemmed mostly from various misunderstandings
of the recent performance of some macroeconomic variables, from an insuffi-
cient understanding of the structural reform process that began to build mo-
mentum after mid-1985, as well as from the faiture to recognize the extent to
which the external conditions have deteriorated lately for Mexico, thus
impairing the effectiveness of its domestic policies.



- 53 -
NEICO - GOVERNINT'S STATMENNT OF                                          ANN y
FOREIGN TRADE POLICY   (Jne 19, 1986)                               Page r2of
Perhaps the most relevant aspect is the balance of payments adjust-
ment.   The improvement in the country's external accounts during 1983-85
should be an undisputed fact. Over thls period, the trade account accumulat-
ed a surplus of US$35 billion, while the current account registered a surpluo
of US$10 billion, in spite of the size of the interest bill on the foreign
debt. The adjustment implied by these figures can be better assessed in the
light of the experience of previous years--in 1981, for instance, the current
account deflcit reached US$12.5 billion--and if it is remembered that not
even during the years of price and financial stability in the late 50's and
the 60's was it possible to achieve positive balances in either account.
It is sometimes stated that the praiseworthy efforts of 1983-84
were spoiled by a slackening of the external adjustment during 1985.  It is
true that both trade and current accounts, as well as international reserves,
deteriorated over this last year, but to a significant extent this occurred
because of conditions well beyond the influence of domestic economic policy.
Oil exports, for one thing, suffered a 10% decrease in average price and a 6%
drop in volume during 1985. The fall in oil revenues alone explains one-half
of the decrease experienced by the current account surplus during 1985.
Exports of other primary products, whose performance is determined more by
external than by internal variables, also suffered a setback.   The only
factor Influenced by domestic policies contributing significantly to a sal-
ler trade balance was the rapid growth of merchandise imports by the private
sector--mostly intermediate and investment goods-a situation that did not
appear alarming, given that imports had registered very low levels for three
consecutive years.
More importantly, however, the Government adopted firm policies in
reaction to the weakening balance of payments.  On the one hand, these in-
cluded exchange rate and other short-term policy adjustments.   On the other
hand, they involved also structural reforms, especially an acceleration of
the Government 's long-term program of trade liberalization and export
promotion.
The Government intends to continue the process of trade liberaliza-
tion, despite the adverse external trade conditions faced by Mexico as a re-
sult of the fall in oil prices. But the Government is acutely aware that its
trade policy objectives cannot be achieved unless there is an appropriate
macropolicy context in support of trade liberalization, combined with indus-
trial and other sectoral reconversion efforts and a supportive external fi-
nancing program. The following sections will spell out each of these inter-
related policy and financing objectives and requirements.
The Trade Policy Program for 1986-88
The current recovery in manufactured exports is occurring upon
structural bases that are different from those prevailing in the past.  In
compliance with the National Development Plan in effect since mid-1983, the
Mexican Government adopted throughout 1985 a number of measures to liberalize
a historically protectionist trade regime and thus reduce the consequent



- 54 -
MEXICO - GOVERNMENT'S STATEMENT OF                                         ANNEX IV
FOREIGN TRADE POLICY   (June 19, 1986)                                Page 3 of 18
anti-export bias that has prevailed in the Mexican economy for many decades.
Furtlhermore, in order to accelerate the effect of opening up the economy in
the export sector, a number of measures have been taken to make sure that all
potential exporters have access to inputs at international prices.   To this
end, a modern drawback system was established in April 1985, and the system
of temporary imports for exporters has been repeatedly subject to further
liberalization.   It is also important to mention that Mexico last November
initiated formal negotiations to become a member of the GATT.
Strategic Reform Objectives of Commercial Policy.   The principles
and objectives of Mexico's foreign trade policy have been clearly set forth
in published plans and programs, which indicate the strategy to be followed
to regain the ability to grow and improve the quality of development.
These documents recognize that the import-substitution model used
as a development strategy for the Mexican economy for more than four decades
has been exhausted.   While  this model  led to rapid  industrialization and
raised employment and output, it also produced side effects that gradually
made it rigid and impracticable, caused distortions in resource allocation,
discouraged economic efficiency, induced high external dependence, and pro-
moted both higher costs and higher profits for domestically-oriented produc-
tion to the detriment of production for export.
in view of this, the present Administration adopted a strategy of
economic reorganization and structural change designed to eliminate rigidi-
ties in the economy, while achieving sustained growth.  In the foreign trade
area, the structural change is intended to ensure that non-oil exports will
cover an increasing share of the import requirements of the Mexican economy.
The National Development Plan (PND), the National Program for
Industrial Promotion and Foreign Trade (PRONAFICE), and the Comprehensive
Export Promotion Program (PROFIEX) are mutually complementary, and all of
them agree that the primary objective of Mexico's foreign trade will be to
balance, expand, and diversify commercial relations on the basis of continu-
ally expanding non-oil exports and efficient selective substitution of im-
ports. A major feature of the policy to be pursued is a change away from an
import substitution strategy aimed at the domestic market and based on a
rigid protectionist structure, towards a policy of export promotion and
greater external competitiveness, through a gradual opening up to foreign
trade.
To achieve these objectives, protection levels must gradually be
rationalized in order to expose Mexican industry to increased competition
from abroad.  This will induce it to make the structural adjustments neces-
sary to improve the utilization of available resources and foster the crea-
tion of a productive system capable of competing with foreign goods on a firm
footing.
The policy of rationalization of protection is aimed at reducing
protection levels, avoiding the anti-export bias of the past, and establish-
ing a policy structure based mainly on tariffs, rather than import licenses,



- 55 -
NEXICO - COVEENHENT'S STATEHENT OF                                       ANNX IV
FOREIGN TRADE POLICY   (June 19, 1986)                              Page 4 of 18
and thus permitting more precise measurement of the actual degree of protec-
tion granted.
Evaluation of Trade Policy under the Present Administration.   In
late 1982 the economic crisis made it necessary to adopt drastic macro-
economic adjustment measures, including exchange devaluations, austerity in
public expenditure, and widespread import controls.
In these circumstances, trade policy instruments continued to be
applied during 1983 with the main objectives of correcting the imbalances of
the balance of payments, rationing the use of foreign exchange, and keeping
the productive system in operation.  This was sought essentially through the
use of import licenses and exchange controls. In addition, tariffs were set
with a view to reducing their inflationary impact,  as reflected  in the
decline in the average tariff by 3 percentage points between 1982 and 1983,
to 24Z.
Starting in 1984 with the publication of PRONAFICE, the design of
foreign trade policy began to focus on a rationalization of protection. The
process of replacing import licenses by tariffs was begun, and during the
year the license requirement was abolished for 2,844 tariff items (about a
third of the total number of items), representing 16.5% of total import value
and comprising mainly needed industrial inputs not produced domestically.
In addition, tariff policy also aimed at reducing the dispersion of
tariffs, preventing price speculation in the domestic market, and simplifying
tariff application by cutting the number of rates.  By end-1984, the number
of tariff rates had declined from 13 to 10, and tariff dispersion had fallen
from 23.8 to 22.5.
Although these measures had only limited impact in changing the
production structure during 1984--as the elimination of import licenses
during that year was aimed primarily at reactivating the productive system,
they did lay the foundations for a more intensive application of the policy
in 1985.
The two funtdamental measures toward rationalizing protection in
1985 were the elimination of import licenses and the tariff reorganization.
The original intention was to phase out the control of items that had not
been decontrolled in 1984 on three different dates:   727 items, accounting
for 10.3% of total imports, would remain under control until October 31,
1985; 3,508 items, representing 35.1% of import value, would be decontrolled
by October 31, 1986; and, finally, 965 items, with a 37.1% share in import
value, would remain controlled until October 31, 1989.
Nonetheless, these targets were accelerated on July 25, 1985.  At
present only 391 tariff items of those which were to remain subject to con-
trol until October 31, 1985 and 1986, and representing 12% of overall
imports,  still remain under control.    Also,  only  414 of the  965 items



- 56 -
NEXICO - GOVERUIENT' S STATIENT OF                                        ANNEK IV
FOREIGN TRADE POLICY   (June 19, 1986)                              Page 35oTf T
originally scheduled for decontrol in October 1989 still remain under con-
trol, representing 232 of import value.   This means that 551 items in this
group have been exempted from the license requirement.
These July 25 adjustments were made in order to face the growing
deterioration of the domestic and external situation, and thus to avold
increasing inflationary pressures and improve the export capacity of Mexican
industry.  To this end, the number of decontrolled items was increased to
7,159 (90% of all items), which account for 62% of total import value Cbased
on 1984 figures).   This means that the protection system has now shifted
towards the use of tariffs, which apply to goods ranging from raw materials
to highly processed products manufactured by both public and private
enterprises.
The liberalization dld not extend to all General Import Tariff
(TIGI) items.  A total of 909 items still remain subject to the import li-
cense requirement, due to the national interest in protecting certain sectors
and preventing wasteful imports of luxury goods.
The substitution of tariffs for licenses was accompanied by the
adoption of generally higher tariff rates, to avoid a sudden and disruptive
removal of protection and instead phase in the tariff gradually, after more
than 40 years of quantitative restrictions.  In net terms, tariffs have been
increased to date for 1,252 items (2,036 tariffs were raised and 784 were
lowered), but only 19% of total imports in value terms have been affected, as
is reflected in the fact that the import-weighted average tariff increased
from 8.6% to 12.3X.
The tariffs in effect for about 95% of TIGI items are thought ade-
quate to the structure established on the basis of the criteria adopted until
now (degree of processing, kind of product, and priority of use or destina-
tion) to begin the programmed tariff reduction.   This means that a small
amount of tariff reorganization still remains to be completed.  The current
structure should be seen as a tool that has been useful for the stage of
transition from licenses to tariffs.
Through rate unification and the elimination of the tariff "saw,"
these changes yielded a more homogenous tariff structure, but did not alter
the tariff dispersion.   In addition, they resulted in a broader use of the
25% and 40% levels, whose coverage increased from 1,946 items in January 1985
to 3,117 items today, representing a change of from 7% to 23% in value terms.
The progress made under the protection rationalization policy in
July 1985 means that the greatest possible trade openness has been achieved
for now. Since that time, adjustments have been made to eliminate inconsis-
tencies and discrepancies that went unnoticed at the time tariffs were sub-
stituted for import licenses.
These recent adjustments have further reduced the number of items
subject to the import license requirement (818), bringing their share in



- 57 -
NYIco -             Ioum su   STTEmns    OF AoNun  IV
FOREIGN TRADE POLICY   (June 19, 1986)                               Page 6 of ii
total import value down to 35%. Also, the increase in the 25% and 40% tariff
rates was offset by eliminating the 75% rate and reducing the use of the 100%
rate.
After these changes, the TIGI now contains 8,100 items, distributed
among 9 tariff rates.   Items subject to tariffs ranging from 0% to 25%
account for 86% of total import value, as reflected In the low weighted
tariff (12%).  The largest rate classes in terms of number of items are the
10%, 25%, and 40% groups, which together Include 78X of all items.  It is
worth noting that although the 40% tariff Includes 23% of all Items, it
covers only 9% of total import value.
Guiding Principles of Trade Policy.  The goal of having the Mexican
economy penetrate world markets by promoting its productive efficiency has
been retained for the period 1986-88.  Despite an unfavorable international
environment, especially as regards the oil market, it has been decided to
continue making structural changes that strengthen the country's development
capeacity.   Therefore, firm action will continue to be taken  to make the
industrial structure more efficient, rationalize the excessive level of
protection, and increase the export capacity.
As the President of the Republic stated in this regard in his
message to the Nation on February 21, 1986:
... Domestic economic policy cannot change either in its basic
objectives or in its adopted strategy.  Both before and after
the oil crisis, the basic problems of our economy remain the
same.   The additional obstacles we are meeting today do not
change the nature of those problems.   ... We are still faced
with the need to adjust the economy by making in-depth struc-
tural changes to strengthen our development capacity."
"We will continue to pursue our firm efforts to rehabilitate
the public finances, to make the industrial system more effi-
cient, to rationalize the excessive level of protection, to
increase the Nation's capacity to generate foreign exchange
through diversified exports and enhanced tourism, to make firm
progress in decentralization, and to raise productivity in
agriculture,  fishing,  forestry,  mining,  and  the  service
sectors, that is, in transportation, commerce, the financial
system, and, of course, the government itself."
With these goals in mind, further progress will be made in the
rationalization of the protection system through the use of tariffs instead
of quantitative restrictions, seeking to increase exposure to external
competition and to reduce the bias against exports and primary activities.
To ensure continued progress of the protection rationalization
policy, gradual, transparent, nondiscriminatory measures are planned both in
the area of tariffs and in the intention to retain only a minimum of quanti-
tative restrictions.



- 58 -
MEXICO - GOVERWNENT'S STTDEMNT OF                                        ANNEU IV
FOREIGN TRADE POLICY   (Jue 19, 1986)                               Page 7 of 18
The gradualism of the program is designed to reduce the uncertainty
caused by sudden changes and to grant industry enough time to make the
adjustments needed to adapt to the new conditions of openness to inter-
national competition.
In addition, incentives must be made more uniform, especially by
means of a tariff structure containing fewer rates and less dispersion,
through the adoption of general tariff levels for categories of goods based
on their type, degree of processing, etc. To this end, the trade policy will
focus basically on the objectives of industrial protection, operating effi-
ciency of the productive system, and increased export generation.
As indicative guidelines for policy, the Government will regularly
monitor levels and dispersion of effective protection, with a view to reduc-
ing the differential between maximum and minimum rates.
The tariff levels will determine the level of protection deemed
desirable for a given domestic line of production and will be equal for
products having the same characteristics.   Producers and sectors deemed of
priority to the economy and requiring greater protection for a time in order
to become established in accordance with current development programs will be
granted this protection in the form of temporary tariff surcharges not
exceeding 50% of the basic tariff rate, thus favoring the use of transparent
tariff protection over quantitative restrictions.
To back up the protection given by the tariff, it is necessary to
continue applying mechanisms to prevent unfair external competition from
affecting the protection structure, damaging domestic industry, and distort-
ing the application of the trade policy.
Trade Policy Measures for 1986-88.  In his message of February 21,
the President of the Republic also announced 10 points that will support the
economic policy measures taken to deal with the Nationls present situation.
One of them is that "we will continue to go further ln revising the protec-
tion system through additional progress in tariff policy and resorting as
little as possible to quantitative restrictions." As he also noted, "We will
continue negotiations for accession to GATT, with a view to having a broad
framework in which to promote our trade relations with the rest of the
world."
During the period 1986-88, the protection rationalization policy
will continue to be consistent, with the objective of gradually opening the
Mexican economy to the rest of the world, while avoiding exposing the produc-
tive system to sudden shocks from foreign competition that would cause
unemployment or discourage investment. More precisely, this policy will help
strengthen the export capacity of domestic industry, mobilize a larger share
of existing productive resources, and make more efficient use of them.
The trade policy measures adopted in 1985 represented a significant
qualitative change in the system of protection, as never before had so large
a proportion of total imports been exempt from licenses as today.

The potential for continuing the substitution of tariffs for import
licenses in the short term will depend on a careful selection of specific
sectors and products whose imports must be handled under the import license
system for structural or legal reasons--whether because of economic and
social priority (as in the case of some agrlcultural products) or because of
public interest or strategic considerations (as in the case of products harm-
ful to health, arms and munitions, and the petroleum sector, among others).
These reasons are discussed in the letter of instructions for
accession to GATT, written by the President of the Republic to the Secretary
of Commerce and Industrial Development on November 22, 1985, which states:
"The protocol of accession must be fully consistent with the
provisions of the Political Constitution of United Mexican
States and of all other laws safeguarding the effectiveness
and exercise of the Nation's sovereignty.  Our sovereignty
over natural resources, and energy resources ln particular,
must be fully respected during the negotiations.  Products
affecting national security or public health, as well as
agricultural products sensitive to the national economy,
should not be considered in the negotiations."
Import licenses are now no longer required for 90.0% of TIGI items,
covering  65.2% of  total  imports.    As  for  the  agricultural  sector  in
particular, 85.0% of its tariff items do not require import licenses, and the
great mejority of agricultural imports still covered by licenses are duty-
free basic necessities of the population.   Even so, further progress can be
made in abolishing import licenses, especially in the capital goods sector.
As regards Mexico's entry into GATT, the Presidernt's instructions
state thtc "Mexico must play an active role in international economic nego-
tiations" and that 'it is in the national interest to initiate proceedings
leading towards Mexico's accession to the General Agreement on Tariffs and
Trade."
This decision is founded on four basic reasons mentioned in the
same document: "... the General Agreement on Tariffs and Trade offers Mexico
not only the possibility of better access for its exports, but also the
provisions necessary to protect its productive system and employment;" "
Mexico is offered one more instrument for the peaceful resolution of disputes
and for the defense of our commercial interests;" and " ... that Mexico make
use of the possibility of actively participating in this forum for purposes
of its own industrial reconversion process."
The work of the Commission on Tariffs and Foreign Trade Restric-
tions will focus on continuing the rationalization of the structure of
protection in a way that will bring the operating environment of Mexican
industry closer to that of other countries at a similar level of development.

The prior import license system will be reviewed twice a year to
eliminate quantitative control. to the greatest  practicable extent.   This
review will exclude Items pertaining to the agricultural, petroleum, pharma-
ceutleals, autoootlve and computer sectors, as well as products harmful to
health or strategic to national defense, since these are covered by specific
legislation and involve speclal economic and social considerations.
Also, import licenses for luxury consumer goods cannot be abolished
at this  time, due  to the  political  impact and the scarcity of foreign
exchange in the current economic situation.   Nevertheless, prohibitions on
Imports of some consumer goods will be eliminated during 1986 and replaced by
quotas, which would be enlarged over time, If conditions permit.
A preliminary estimate shows that approximately 176 items--mainly
capital goods, chemicals, rubber and plastic products, edible oils and fats,
and paper and cardboard--ay be exempted from the license requirement by not
later than September 30, 1987.
A program of gradual tariff reduction, generally appllcable to all
sectors but allowing for the possibility of differential treatment for duly
accredited sectors, is the most appropriate way to pursue the protection
rationalization policy and to eliminate the excessive protection now granted
to certain sectors.

For productive industries or specific sectors deemed of priority or
special importance to the national economy under present or future developr
ment programs and which require additional protection for a time in order to
get established, such protection will be granted through temporary tariff
surcharges not to exceed 50% of the base tariff rate set for the product.
Similarly, the Federal Government reserves the right to establish a 50% base
tariff rate, under exceptional domestic or international economic circum-
stance, for certain activities justifying a special level of protection.
The targets set for further sabstitution of licenses by tariffs and
for the tariff reduction program were made public on April 30, 1986 in order
to remove uncertainty and allow the productive system to make investment,
production, and pricing plans, and to give it adequate time to make the
adjustments necessary to face competition from abroad.
The tariff reduction process now begun will be continued in a
gradual and homogenous manner, in order to prevent speculative practices and
the development of expectations that could hinder its success.  Moreover,
preannouncement of these measures and their timing reduces uncertainty in the
productive system and provides it with sufficient time to make the necessary
adjustments in its production plans. The time required for such adjustments,
including acquisition of machinery and know-how, plant design, relocation,
and the like, usually ranges from two to five years.

FOREIGN TRADE POLICY   (June 19, 1986)                              Page 11 of 18
A first step in the tariff reduction process was the publication of
a Presidential Decree implementing actions scheduled for April 30, 1986.  It
introduced changes affecting 4,580 tariff items, of whlch 4,282 are tariff
reductions.
In general, items previously subject to the 20%, 25%, and 30% rates
had their tariff level cut by 2.5 percentage points.  In addition, the 100Z
rate was abolished, and all items previously subject to it were reduced by up
to 45% and brought together with those formerly subject to the 50Z tariff.
The decree also did away with the 5% rate.  Of the 325 items that
were dutied at this rate, 297 were raised to 10%, and the remainder was
exempted from the import tax.  Thus, the relief process excluded those items
currently exempted from tariff and those dutied at the 10% rate.
The 4,580 items included in the published decree cover 36.1% of
total imports, and, as a result of these changes, there will be a decrease of
slightly less than 2 percentage points in both the average tariff and the
tariff dispersion, while the tariff weighted by 1985 imports will decline by
one-half percentage point.
Official import reference prices will continue to be used as until
now to combat unfair foreign trade practices until December 1987, s0 as to
avoid the risk of damage to local industry from such practices, while setting
up the data bank and infrastructure needed to bring into full operation the
new countervailing duty mechanism established by the Foreign Trade Law. This
data bank will be created to collect the international experience needed to
permit implementation of the countervailing duty mechanism, with the support
of the services of an internationally reputed firm in the area of customs
valuation.
The existence of unfair trade practices in the international market
justifies retaining a streamlined, obstacle-free handling of the reference
price system, while rapidly implementing the countervailing duty mechanism
set up by the new Foreign Trade Law of 1985. To this end, the implementing
regulations for this Law will be put into effect before September 1986.
At present, 1,171 tariff  items are  subject  to official import
reference prices, covering 8.4% of total 1985 imports.   This is a smaller
number than the 1,348 items covered by Import reference prices at the start
of 1985.
Import reference prices will be reviewed every three months with a
view to eliminating redundant protection, such as in the case of items simul-
taneously subject to import licenses, high tariffs, and reference prices, or
the case of recorded import prices that exceed the official reference price.
In normal circumstances, reference prices will not at any time apply to more
than 10% of the total value of imports. Moreover, the reference prices will
be analyzed in the second half of 1986 in the light of progress achieved in
implementing the countervailing duty mechanisms, to establish the possibility
of a gradual disappearance of reference prices as this new system comes Into
application.
