Lastly, during the second halE of 1986, Mexico will announce its
intention to adapt its nomenclature to that of the Harmonized Commodity
Description and Coding Sytem, replaning the current Customs Cooperation
Council Nomenclature, as from January 1, 1988, as befits its position as
candidate for accession to GATT and the fact that the future rounds of multi-
lateral negotiations within GATT will take place under the nomenclature of
the Harmonized System.  The new system will not only permit better identifi-
cation, but will also facilitate trade flows with the rest of the world.
The Hacropolicy Framework for Trade Liberalization
As early as December 1984, the daily devaluation of the controlled
exchange rate was increased--this rate applies to about 80% of all foreign
exchange transactions,  including all merchandise trade.   The procedure was
repeated after the price of crude fell in February 1985.
A few months later even more drastic measures were implemented. On
July 11, the Mexican commercial banks were allowed to operate in the free
foreign exchange market through their own bureaus of exchange.   This action
sought to broaden the free foreign exchange market in order to make it less
prone to excessive instability.   Less than two weeks later, the controlled
exchange rate was devaluated by 20%, and a new system of managed float--
instead of the preannounced rate of crawl--was implemented.
These policy adjustments have enabled the free and controlled
exchange rates to exercise a more flexible and timely equilibrating function
of the external sector of the economy.   In real terms,  the free and con-
trolled rates depreciated significantly during 1985.   Not surprisingly,  in
view of the evolution of oil prices, real devaluation has continued during
the present year. Consequently, as of the end of last April, the controlled
exchange rate--the relevant one for foreign trade--had been devalued in real
terms by 20% as compared to the end of 1983, when it was correctly deemed
undervalued by most analysts, as proven by the fact that during 1984 the Bank
of Mexico's international reserves grew by US$3.2 billion.
Enhanced flexibility has also narrowed the differential between the
free and controlled  rates.   At  the beginning  of  the  two-tier system  in
December 1982, the differential was as wide as 50%.   During the first five
months of 1986 it averaged only 2.5%.
The reaction of the trade and service flows to the adjustment of
real exchange rates and other macroeconomic variables is already evident.
Exports of manufactured goods grew at an annual rate of 10% during the last
four months of 1985, and at the rate of 17.5% in the first quarter of this
year. During the latter period, the value of merchandise imports has fallen
at an annual rate of 6.5%.  Value added by in-bond industries and net income
from tourism are also expanding very dynamically.
It is the Government's intention to maintain the enhanced flexi-
bility of exchange rate policy, with a view to maintaining export competi-
tiveness and adequate reserves and avoiding the reimposition of quantitative
import restrictions for balance of payments purposes.



- 64 -
MNICO - GOVERNNEUT'S ST&TENEWE OF                                        ANNE   IV
VOEEIGN TRADE POLICY   (June 1_9 1986)                              Page TS13Tf
Another major component of the macropolicy framework that is needed
to ensure the success of the trade liberalization process is adequate fiscal
policy.  This is another lssue on which international perceptions have been
adverse.   It is widely believed that a relaxation of the fiscal adjustment
effort of 1983-84 began to occur In the last months of 1984.
Indeed, the conventional fiscal targets were significantly over-
ridden in 1985.
Last year, the target for the overall fiscal deficit as a propor-
tion of GOP called for a maximum of 5.1Z.  In practice, that proportion
reached 1OZ.   The  fall in oil income,  the difficulties involved in the
further reduction of public expenditure after two years of strong fiscal
austerity--a circumstance severely complicated by the September earthquakes,
and  the  persistence  of  higher-than-expected  inflation,  which  greatly
increased interest payments on the domestic public debt: all these are among
the determining factors in the adverse budgetary outcome of 1985.
The task of restructuring public finances in Mexico is far from
over, and the Mexican Government-notwithstanding adverse internal and exter-
nal conditions--continues working very actively to that end.  At the same
time, however, it is believed that by focussing on the overall fiscal
deficit, observers of the Mexican economy may overlook the magnitude of the
underlying fiscal adjustment accomplished since 1983.
The overall fiscal deficit, as a measure of the pressure exerted by
the public sector on aggregate demand and the external balance, has a funda-
mental  deficiency  under  high-inflation  conditions.    To  elucidate  this
problem, it may be useful to recall that nominal interest payments on any
credit have two components: first, the real payment; and second, the nominal
payment necessary to keep the real value of the credit intact. Economically
speaking, the inflation induced component constitutes a payment of principal,
not of real intetest, and it tends to be many times greater than the real
interest component when inflation is high.
Therefore, in an inflationary economy a serious implication of this
phenomenon is that very substantial payments of principal on the public
debt--hidden as interest payments-are computed as part of public current
expenditure.   Consequently, the overall fiscal deficit, that in principle
should measure only net borrowing requirements, in an inflationary situation
includes financing undertaken simply to roll over past liabilities.   This
procedure is as misleading as if, at the corporate level, payments of debt
were computed as current outlays.
Consequently, it has been suggested by various analysts, and put
into practice in some instances, that a better concept of public sector
deficit is one that excludes the inflation-induced component of interest
payments.   This concept, known as the operational deficit, does provide a
true measure of the net additional borrowing requirements of the public
sector and its size determines the evolution of the stock of public debt in
real terms.



- 65 -
HIICO - COVUIIT'S STATKUT OF                                              AnN  IV
FOEEIGI TRADE POLICY   (Jum. 19, i986)                               Page 14 of 18
It is oE interest to note how this operational deficit has evulved
in Mexico during the last few years.   It averaged about 1% of GDP in the
period 1978-80, and jumped to 3.62 in 1981-82.  Over the period 1983-85, it
has averaged less than 1% of CDP?  We should also add that as a consequence
of the oil price drop a number of measures have already been undertaken by
the Mexican Administration to reduce real government expenditure further and
increase non-oil fiscal revenues, so that the operational deficit substan-
tially disappears in 1986.  This result should be reviewed in the light of
the oil export earnings to be lost during the current year estimated at about
5Z ot GDP.   In other words,  had  the loss not occurred,  Mexico would be
running a substantial operational surplus.
Logically, the counterpart of the 1983-85 performance of the opera-
tional deficit was a reduction in the real size of the public debt.  During
this period, both the external public debt, in constant value dollars, and
the internal public debt, in constant value pesos, diminished by almost 5%.
While the operational deficit more accurately reflects fiscal ef-
foL by the Government, nonetheless the Government fully recognizes that it
is the full nominal deficit that must be financed and that it therefore
remains critical to the objective of reducing inflation.
The Government's fiscal program for 1986-88,i/ as detailed in
Table 1, presently envisages substantial further fiscal adjustment effort to
bring inflation under control and to reduce overall public sector borrowing
requirements to less than 42 of GDP by 1988.
Industrial and Other Sectoral Reconversion ProsraoJ
The process of gradual opening of the Mexican economy will bring
about a major shift in the structure of incentives and relative prices, and
will therefore require commensurate adjustments in the patterns of produc-
tion, investment, and employment.  To facilitate these adjustments,  the Gov-
ernment has begun to implement a series of programs of reconversion of the
principal sectors that will be affected by the change in the trade policy
environment. The industrial sector is a primary area of reconversion efforts
now under way, but the agricultural sector will be a major beneficiary of the
reduced anti-export bias and will also need reconversion assistance in order
to exploit the expected new opportunities for exports.
The Government's industrial reconversion strategy is aimed at both
large parastatal enterprises and support for private industrial firms in need
of adjustment assistance.   In the parastatal sector, the Government has al-
ready implemented programs to restructure FERTIMEX (fertilizers), SIDERKEX
(steel), railroads,  shipbuilding, and sugar.  Additional restructuring pro-
grams are under review for other industrial subsectors and the relevant para-
statals.   Some 278 smaller  parastatal  firms are  in the process of being
merged, privatized, or closed, with agreements already completed for the sale
of 112 firms.
I/         It should be emphasized that the specific quantitative targets of
the Government's present fiscal program are continually under
review and may be subject to further adjustments.



- 6b -
MEXICO - GOVEENDRIT'S STATPEMNT OF                                          ANKI IV
FOREIGN TRADE POLICY   (June 19, 1986)                                 Page 15 of 18
In the agricultural sector, the Government has progressed consider-
ably in the restructuring of CONASUPO (farm and food subsidies), and plans
are under review for the restructuring of BANRURAL (the Government rural
bank).  In addition, the private sector is increasingly participating in the
importation and commercialization of some agricultural goods that were previ-
ously subject to state monopoly.
Internal trade and price controls are being splectively relaxed or
eliminated in both the industrial and agricultural sectors, and further
progress in this area is expected to accompany the external liberalization
process, so as to facilitate the gradual conversion of domestic and
international prices.
The External Fimancin Progra for 1986-88
As is well known, Mexico is presently subject to an external shock
that could easily prove to be the biggest in at least half a century.  Last
January, oil prices started to drop sharply.   The current average price for
Mexican crude is about 53% less  than it was at the end of 1985.   Export
volume has also decreased significantly.   if the present situation continues
during 1986, the country will lose about US$7.5 billion in oil exports
revenue compared to last year and almost US$10 billioe. with respect to 1983.
A conservative estimate would put LU.e size of this external shock in the
vicinity of 5% of GDP.
Considering the dimension of the oil shock, it is obvious that the
country's current account perspectives are somewhat adverse for the near
future, in spite of all efforts to promote non-traditional exports and sub-
stitute imports.  A loss of revenue as big as the one caused by the collapse
of oil prices cannotp in the short-run, be fully compensated by increased
exports and reduced imports alone.
For this reason three months apo the Mexican authorities started
consultations with the country's creditors, their respective monetary author-
ities and with international agencies, to arrange a suitable financing pack-
age as quickly as possible.   Such a request  is warranted not only by the
gravity of the situation, but also by the way in which the economy was ad-
justed during the three previous years, is being adjusted at the present
time, and shall continue to be adjusted in the future.
Relying partially on a moderate increase in external indebtness to
absorb the oil shock cannot be considered an unwise policy, if one takes into
account that Mexico, for three consecutive years, far from increasing, has
reduced the size of its external debt  in real terms.   In support of this
view, consider that during the period 1983-85 the net flow of external
indebtedness--inclusive of changes in suppliers credits--was of US$3.0
billion, which is only slightly more than one-fifth of what would have been
required to keep the total external debt at the same level of December of
1982 in terms of constant value dollars.2/
2/    Using the average variations in consumer prices of industrialized coun-
tries.



- 67 -
MExICO - GOVMENT'S STATUMENT OF                                           ANNE  IV
FOREIGN TRADE POLICY   (June 19, 1986)                               Page 16 of 18
The net external financing requirements3/ for 1986 are currently
estimnated at US$5.3 billion (Table 2), compared to the original (October
1985)  estimate  of US$4.0 billion.   The original estimate was based on an
expected average price of crude of US$22.50 per barrel for 1986, whereas the
revised financing program assumes an average price of US$12.30.  The original
projection of total oil revenue  for 1986 was US$13.6  billion,  down  from
US$14.8 billion in 1985.   Instead, the revised estimate of total oil export
revenue for 1986 is US$6.2 billion, a decline from the original projection of
US$7.4 billion.   Thus,  while expected oil export revenue has declined by
US$7.4 billion, the revised projections of financing requirements have
increased by only US$1.3 billion.   Evidently, a significant part of the oil
revenue shortfall is being compensated for by policy adjustments and domestic
effort to increase non-oil exports, reduce imports, and economize on inter-
national reserves, added to the drop in nominal international interest rates.
Net financing needs decline gradually to US$4.9 billion in 1987 and
to US$4.5 billion in 1988, on the assumption of continued domestic effort and
an international price of crude that improves slowly from US$12.3 per barrel
in 1986 to US$13.0 in 1987, and US$15.0 in 1988.
Conclusion
The present Mexican economic situation does not fulfill the favor-
able expectations that Mexico and the internatinnal financial community had a
couple of years ago. The achievements, however, are by no means negligible,
if seen in the context of the extremely adverse external conditions.   The
Mexican terms of trade have deteriorated by 452 since December 1982, whereas
real interest rates on our foreign debt, although lower than they were, have
remained high by any historical standard.
It is a fact that Mexico has paid a high price for having to adjust
under  such difficult  external conditions.   Real GDP per capita and real
average wages in 1986 will certainly be substantially below the levels
attained five years ago.  It cannot be taken lightly that the deterioriation
in the Mexican standard of living has coexisted with a situation in which the
process of servicing responsibly our foreign debt has implied an enormous
transfer of resources to the creditor countries.
Fiscal discipline and structural adjustment are not new concepts to
Mexico. Making them part of the strategy to solve the crisis that erupted in
1982 was a sovereign decision adopted more than three years ago.  In living
up to this decision, the Mexican Government has undertaken very courageous
and unprecedented actions.   However it is expected  that creditors,  their
respective monetary authorities, and the international agencies lend their
support to the resolution of this problem as well, and thereby contribute
significantly to the realization of these goals.
3/    The specific quantitative targets of the Government's presently pro-
posed external financing plan are continually under review and may be
subject to further adjustments.

HEXICO
MEMORANDUM ON METHODOLOGY
MEASURENINT OF TRADE POLICY TARGETS AND OBJECTIVES
(June 25, 1986)
DOMESTIC OUTPUT WEIGETING FACTORS FOR IMPORT TARIFF ITEKS
1.     Background
1.        Traditionally,  the Government  of Mexico has  regulated  imports
through a combined system of tariffs and permits.  The degree of restric-
tiveness of this system has undergone variations, principally in terms of
the balance of payments situation.   At the end of the 70s,  for example,
when there was a hard currency surplus because of income from oil exports,
import restrictions were relatively relaxed.  But in September 1982, when
the country experienced the greatest payments crisis in its modern history,
all  imports were subjected  to the requirement of prior permits, and no
permits were granted unless strictly necessary to keep productive capacity
in operation.
2.       In recent years,  there has been growing recognition that import
restrictions are not the most appropriate instrument to resolve problems
relating to the balance of payments.  Even if this policy may be the most
effective one to bring about a swift reduction in the outflow of reserves,
it creates a discriminatory, discretionary, and intransparent structure of
incentives to internal production, which, in the long run, has resulted in
an overprotected and unresponsive production system that is not competitive
with foreign producers.
3.       These are the chief considerations that in July 1985 prompted a
significant change in trade policy.   For the first time in the country's
history a significant liberalization of the import regime was decreed at a
time when  the balance  of payments  prospects  were  far from reassuring.
Before the reforms of the system, 4,513 out of 8,077 items were subject to
the prior permit requirement.  Of the 4,153, items, 3,604 were liberalized
on July 25, leaving only 909 under control, through which 38% of the value
of total imports (1984 figures) were channeled.
4.      At the same time, it was realized that the most appropriate way of
achieving equilibrium in the external balance did not consist of restrict-
ing imports through permits and tariffs, but in adjusting the exchange
rate. This was made explicit with the devaluation of the peso simultaneous
to the freeing of imports and the announcement of the official intention to
pursue further trade liberalization.



- 71 -
MEXICO - MEMORANDUM ON METHODOLOGY                                      ANNEX V
(June 25, 1986)                                                     Page 2 of 8
5.      The Government's program of trade liberalization calls for new ways
of measuring the degree to which the import regime has been liberalized, in
order to better design, monitor, and give increased trinsparency to the
Government's trade policy targets and objectives.   This Memorandum des-
cribes some alternatives in this connection, and a system of measurement
based on weighting factore of domestic output is defined.
2.     Measuring Degree of the Openness of the Trade Regime
a.     Simple Averaging
6.     There are several ways of quantifying the advances of the liberali-
zation program.   With respect to control through prior permits, the most
obvious one is to simply report the number of tariff items that are subject
to this restriction.  For example, the reforms of last year freed 3,604 of
the 4,513 controlled items out of a total of 8,077 items, leaving 909
controlled. Similarly, the simple average of the tariff rates of the items
can be used to obtain an indicator of the restrictiveness of tariffs.
1.     However, these methods in no way account for the relative importance
of each of the items, for the same weight is attributed to algae as to
crude oil or to sodium citrate.  It would thus be easy to give the appear-
ance of considerable liberalization in items of little importance in terms
of shares in trade or output.
b.     Weighted Averaging based on Imports
8.      In light of the foregoing,  it is necessary to find a method that
accounts for the relative importance of each item through a system of
weighting  factors.    For  exarple,  one  could  use  the  value  of  imports
channeled through each item during a certain period.  Under this procedure,
the indicator for the coverage of permits is no longer the number of
controlled items, but the share, in the value of imports accounted for by
these  items;  and,  for  the  tariffs,  the  indicator  is  the  average
import-weighted tariff, instead of the unweighted tariff mean.
9.       Unfortunately, weighting by import values has the disadvantage of
underestimating  the restrictiveness  of the regime.   In fact,  the more
restrictive the measures, the fewer imports there will be, so that the
weighting factors of the hardest hit items decrease.   In the extreme case
in which all permit applications  are denied,  no imports  enter  through
controlled items, and the weighting factors of these items will be zero.
Consequently there will be an appearance of complete freeing when a very
restrictive system will actually be in place.
co     Weighted Average based on Domestic Production
10.    To avoid this bias, it is normal practice to use weighting systems
that reflect the relative importance of goods in domestic production.



- 72 -
MExICO - HmxOwwUx ON 0 ETHODOLOGY                                        NSX v
(June 25, 1986)                                                      Page 3 of 8
Instead of asking what is the percentage of imports channeled through items
subject  to permits  or  other  non-tariff  barriers  (NWU),  the  question
becomes:    What  percentage  of domestic  production  is subject  to NTBs.
Likewise, tariffs can be averaged on the basis of domestic production.
11.    It is necessary to mention some disadvantages of weighting based on
domestic production.   The first is of a practical nature and consists of
the statistical work necessary to establish a correspondence between
domestically produced goods on the one hand and tariff items on the other.
12.     Another disadvantage resides in the fact that,  from the point of
view of national accounting, the concept of production has more than one
meaning.   The intermediate inputs elaborated in a factory and then incor-
porated into subsequent production within the same factory are normally not
considered  part  of output  at the national  level.   Thus  output at the
national level depends on the statistical unit of input.   It can happen,
for instance, that the primary smelting of iron or steel appears indepen-
dently in the production accounts, or it can be eliminated as a direct
input in secondary smelting.
13.    Perhaps a greater disadvantage lies in the fact that much of domes-
tic output does not compete directly or indirectly with imports, so that
the regime remains without effect for such goods.   For example, the fact
that crude oil imports are subject to NTBs is a largely institutional
matter and may have little or no protectionist effect. Nevertheless, crude
oil represents 14% of Mexico's tradeable output, for which reason the
percentage subject to permits is commensurately raised by 14 points.
14.    However, other export commodities exist for which a permit or import
tariff is not totally superfluous.   Take,  for example,  coffee and beer,
which are export commodities whose importation is subject to the prior
import requirement.   If imports of these were allowed, other varieties of
coffee and other brands of beer would surely be imported, even though
domestic  equivalents  are  perfectly  competitive  on  foreign  markets.
Therefore, it is not easy to determine for what commodities the NTB regime
is operative and to what extent.
d.     Weighted Average based on YFifl Demud
15.      To avoid  the disadvantages deriving  from  the ambiguity  of the
concept of output in national accounting, weighting factors of tinal demand
can be used in place of those of domestic output.   However,  it must be
pointed out that information seldom exists on the final demand per product
at the required  level of detail.   An approximation can be achieved by
assigning a fixed economic destination (intermediate or final) to each
product of domestic output. But many commodities--the most important among
them-are destined to both intermediate and final demand (sugar, gasoline,
textiles, etc.).



- 73 -
HSKICO - NIORAMDUNI NTODOLOGY                                            AXI    V
(June 251 1986)                                                     Page 4 of 8
e.     WeLghted Average based on Domestic Deomd
16.    To mitigate the bias introduced by the fact that the NTB regime has
little if any impact on the protection of exported or nontraded good., the
weighting factors can be measured in terms of internal demand, i.e., domes-
tic output plus imports minus exports.   This approach would entail some
statistical classification and appraisal difficulties, though these do not
seem insurmountable, but the approach in any event would not be totally
correct for biases.
3.     Estimation of Domestic Output Weighting Factors
17.      The methodology here chosen involves weighting  factors based on
domestic output for approximately 2,500 products encompassing more chan 902
of Mexico's traded goods production.  The manner in which these weighting
factors have been estimated is explained in the methodological appendix.
18.    When these weighting factors are applied to the NTB regime in effect
on March 31, 1986, the result is that 55.02% of domestic output was subject
to prior permits (QRs) and 17.5% to official reference prices (ORPs). The
net MTM coverage (excluding double-counting of items subject to both
Permits and ORPs) is 67.5%.   Siuilarly,  one finds a production-weighted
average tariff rate of 22.7Z, with a standard deviation of 25.6%.
19.    The results, broken down by branch and subbranch, are presented in
the  statistical  appendix  (Annex VIII).   The  tables  in the  statistical
appendix show which sectors are affected by the various types of NTBs and
in which part of their domestic output that impact occurs.  More than half
of QR coverage, for example, is traceable to petroleum and natural gas
(15.9%); agricultura (7.4%); oil refining (4.6%); apparel (2.5%); and the
assembled auto industry (1.9X).  Similarly, the heaviest incidence of ORPs
is found in soft textiles (2.22); the assembled auto industry (1.9%); the
basic iron and steel industry (1.8%); paper and coal (1.2%); and the beer
industry (L.1Z).
20.      The set of domestic output weighting factors makes possible the
immediate estimation of changes in the sectoral incidence of new or reduced
NTBs, as well as the weighted sectoral tariff structure and its spread.



- 74 -
MEXICO - MEMORANDUM ON CETODOLOGY                                        ANNEX V
(APPENDIX)  (June 25, 1986)                                         Poge 5 of 5
MEXICO
METHODOLOGICAL APPENDIX
ESTIMATION OF DOMESTIC-OUTPUT WEIGUTINC FACTORS
1.     Level of Atgregation
21.     The weighting factors were estimated for three levels of aggrega-
tion: (i) branch level (59 branches that produce tradeable goods); (ii)
subbranch  level (157 branches);  and (iii) product  level.   The weighting
factors of the branches and subbranches were calculated based on the value
of output at current prices, reported in the National Accounts of 1983.
2.     Sources of Information
22.     The principal source of information used to estimate the weighting
factors of commodities in the subbranches was the list of Products Obtained
by Type of Activity of the Industrial Census of 1975.  To date there is no
more recent equally detailed information.   This list contains information
on the production value of 9,000 products approximately classified by value
in descending order according to 256 types of activity.
23.    Other sources of information used in the estimates were:  (1) Annual
Industrial Survey of 1983; (ii) Statistical Yearbook of Domestic Agricul-
tural Output of 1981; (iii) Report of Petroleos Mexicanos (PENEX) Activi-
ties for 1984; and (iv) the Input/Output Matrix for 1970.   These sources
were used to estimate weighting factors of the products of branches 1 to 4,
33, 34, and some special subbranches.   For example, in the assembled auto
industry, where a structural change in favor of cars with fewer cylinders
has been carried out, the most recent report of the Industrial Survey,
1983, was used.
3.     Classification of Commodities in Accordance with Tariff Items
24.    As a first step, an attempt was made to determine, for each commod-
ity contained in the lists, the corresponding tariff position.  It should
be pointed out that this involves a classification of commodities towards
the tariff position, not of the tariff positions towards the commodities.
In other words, the commodity remains within the tariff position, but the
coverage of the position may be broader.
25.     It can happen, and in fact it has happened frequently, that dif-
ferent commodities are mapped in one single tariff item.  To avoid as much
as possible such repetitions, commodities that can be classified under dif-
ferent tariff items have been grouped in one single category when the repe-
titions are witlhn the subbranches.   Where repetitions occur within one
branch, but in different subbranches, the commodities involved have usually
been transferred to the subbranch of greatest output.



- 75 -
NKRICO -  fhO MINDWH ON MHTIODOLOCT                                     ANNEX V
(APPlNDIX)  (Juo  25, 1986)                                         page 6 of 8
26.      The repetitions in different branches have not been eliminated.
Another difficulty that has often arisen is that the commodity, or the
description thereof, did not lend itself to classification as a single
item.  For dresses, for example, there are more than five possible items,
depending on whether they are of cotton, synthetic or artificial fibers,
wool, or another fiber.  In such cases, the weight of the weighting factor
was spread over the possible items in subjectLve fashion.  In many cases a
simple pro rating was the only solution.
27.     There were also cases in which an aggregation of tariff items was
applied.  Domestically-produced pigments, for instance, are classified as
75 items, almost all with a tariff rate of 40% and no permit requirement.
To prevent consequent repetition of the product, the items involved were
aggregated according to regime and subsection.
28.     Finally, it must be noted that for a number of products it proved
impossible to find the corresponding item.  Sometimes the reason for this
was that the description of the product was so unclear that its classifi-
cation was impossible; in other cases, too much tie would have been spent
searching.   This applied especially to products of little Importance in
domestic production, which were omitted if the deadline for finding the
item was not met.
29.    In this manner, a total of 2,388 commodities were classified, cover-
ing more than 90% of domestic production. Among them, there are 355 repe-
titions of items, so that, without repetitions, 2,033 tariff items are cov-
ered. However, among these 2,033 items, 8 are added items, which actually
represent 73 individual items, increasing the number of items relresented
to a total of 2,098. The annexes contain the list of items included in the
eight added items.
4.     Weighting Factors
30.    To calculate the ueighting factors of the pr-oducts included, first
their reported production values in the lists were recorded.  Ther these
production values were standardized in terms of the value of the weighting
factor of the corresponding subbranch.
31.    Of course, this procedure assumes that the structure of the included
products is representative of those aot incorporated, which is not entirely
justifiable.  However, it must be borne in mind that the included products
represent more than 90% of total output, so that any possible bias cannot
be very great.
32.     An alternative form sr3gested in order to take account of products
not included consists of:  (i) adding all non-included products for each
st bbranch; (ii) estimating its relative value in the output of the sub-
branch; and (iii) evenly spreading the resulting weighting faccor among all
the items belonging to the subbranch but not present among the items of the
included products.



- 76 -
MEXICO -  MOUANDUN O UTEODOLOGT                                           ANNXI   V
(APPENDIX)  (June 25, lM6G)                                           age. 7 of 8
33.    There are three reasons why much a procedure does not appear advis-
able. The first is that there is no cortainty that the non-included prod-
ucts should not also be classified in items already included; the second is
that no satisfactory classification of items by subbranches exists, and the
third is that, among non-included products, there are many belonging to the
so-called "secondary output," that is to say, products that would be
classified in other subbranches.
5.     Derived Indicators
34.    Four indicators of the protection provided by the import regime were
derived from the weighting factors calculated in the above manner:
a) The percentage of domestic output subject to prior permits;
b) The percentage of domestic output subject to official prices;
c) The average tariff; and
d) The tariff variance (-dispersion squared).
All these  indicators can be calculated by branch, by subbranch for any
aggregation of branches or subbranches, and for the tradeable production of
the whole economy.
35.     The first two indicators are obtained by accumulating the weighting
factors of the products controlled separately. The average tariff and the
tariff  variance  were  calculated  on  the  basis  of  output,  evaluated  at
domestic prices, while the averaging of the tariff rates should ideally be
done with weighting factors for foreign prices. To convert the evaluation
of the foreign price weighting factors, it would be necessary to have a
ratio between domestic and  foreign prices  for all commodities.   In the
absence of this type of information, one can consider the division between
(1 + t) as a Eirst approximation of this conversion.  It is easy to prove
that below this given, the proposed form is equivalent to a direct
averaging with weighting factors for foreign prices.
6.     Tariff Variance
36.      The tariff variance was also obtained with weighting factors at
approximate foreign prices. It should be noted that the total variance can
be broken down into two elements:   the weighted average of the variance
within the sectors and the intersectoral variance of the sectoral
averages. A similar breakdown can be applied at the branch and subbranch
level.
37.     Finally, it must be observed that tariff dispersion is defined as
the square root of the variance:
dispersion   -rvariance

B. Non-financial Relations
VII. Exchange rate arrangement: Since August 5, 1985 two official markets
have been operative in Mexico:  a controlled market covering speci-
fied transactions amounting to about 80% of trade and payments tran-
sactions  and a parallel market.   The controlled market  rate  is
determined under a managed float system guided by a set of indica-
tors, whereas the parallel market rate is established by market
forces.   The controlled market  rate undergoes  daily  unspecified
discreet adjustments.   Parties eligible to utilize the controlled
market (with the exception of office and agencies of the public
administration) can choose to complete any transaction at a "retail
rate" agreed between the parties and the financial institutions
authorized to operate in this market, or at the "equilibriu' ex-
change rate" of the day.  The daily "equilibrium exchange rate" is
determined each day at a fixing session at the central bank, where
representatives of the various financial institutions operating in
the market exchange bids for purchases and sales of foreign ex-
change; the central bank may also submit bids in these sessions.
For transactions exceeding US$50,000, the parties concerned may make
completion of the transaction contingent upon the equilibrium
exchange rate achieving a particular minimum or maximum value. As
of March 1986 the "controlled market equilibrium exchange rate" was
Mex$472.70 per U.S. dollar, and the spread between the exchange
rates in the controlled market and the parallel market was about
2.4X.
VIII. Last Article IV Consultation and Review under Special Facilities: The
laat Article IV consultation and discussions of the 1985 program
were completed by the Executive Board on June 7, 1985 (EBM/85/91).
The relevant supporting documents were SM/85/123, Cor. 1, 5/13/85
and SM/85/148, Cor. 1, 5/23/85.  For consultation purposes, Mexico
is on the 12-month cycle.
IX.   Technical Assistance:  At the request of the Mexican authorities, during
1985 two technical teams from the Fiscal Affairs Department have
provided technical assistance in the fields of direct and indirect
taxation.
