EVOLUTION AND REFORM OF TRADE POLICY
1.              Mexico's  postwar  trade  policy has  been geared toward import
substitution, particularly for the indurtrial sector.  In agriculture, domestic
supply has taken precedence over tra3e, and trade policy has been closely
intertwined with domestic price  controls and  marketing arrangements.   This
chapter describes the changes in Mbxico's trade policy from 1960 to the present,
and analyses the range of policies and instruments that affect industry and
other sectors.  This Annex provides an overview of policies directly affecting
imports and exports, such as tarif±s, import and export restrictions, and other
discretionary controls on trade.
A.    EVOLUTION OF MEXICO'S TRADE POLICY REGIMES
2.        Mexico entered the 1950s with a relatively open economy, characterized
by few quantitative restrictions on imports and low to moderate tariffs.
Prices, however. rose rapidly initiating an inflation-devaluation cycle which
resulted in an increase in the peso-dollar exchange rate from 4.85 in 1945 to
12.50 in September 1954, when it was fixed at a level that would prevail until
September 1976. Although the period under the fLxed exchange rate regime has
been characterized as one of "stabilizing development", rates of domestic
inflation, nevertheless, exceeded those of most of Mexico's major trading
partners, resulting in an appreciation of the real exchange rate.
3.          With the increasing overvaluation of the peso pressures emerged to
increase protection levels.   Tariffs were first raised in 1956 as part of a
general revision of the tariff classification system. The unification of customs
classifications in 1960 provided another opportunity for an increase in tariff
rates, and all tariffs were again increased in 1965. Average tariff protection
increased dramatically from 14.9Z in 1977 to 20.12 in 1978, and then rose
continuously, but at a slower rate, to around 27% in 1982. The dispersion of the
tariff structure (measures by the standard deviation of tariff rates) rose from
11.7% to 19.9%.  Both the average tariff rate and dispersion increased further
until 1982.
4.        While average tariff protection increased from 1965 to 1982, it cannot
fully account for the dramtic increase in effective protection (based on price
comparisons) during this period (see Table VIII.1). Nor can differences in tar-
1ff rates across industries fully explain observed differences in implicit
nominal protection.   Changes in the coverage of quota restrictions, price con-
trols, combined with movements in the exchange rate have been more important
determinants of nominal protection over time. The scope of import controls was
substantially enlarged during the 1960s, with approximately 1000 import catego-
ries per year added to the control list (Table VIII.2).   Controlled imports
represented approximately 602-65Z of total imports throughout the second half of
the decade (See Figure 15 of main text).
5.          In the early 1970u a moderate decline in the percentage of imports
under quota occurred, due to an increased import share of raw materials and
fuels that were not subject to controls. Between 1972 and 1976, however, the



- 81 -
HEXICO - EVOLUTION MED mXzOn OF                                            AnnX VIfI
TmAD. POLICY                                                           Page 2 of 15
coverage of imports by the quota system increased sharply, from 56% to 94%, as
the peso became increasingly overvalued.   The peso devaluation of 1976 was
accompanied by a rollback of the coverage of the quota regime to the levels
which had prevailed  in the  late 1960s.   The Lopez-Portillo administration,
moreovert committed itself to further reductions in the quota regime which were
carried out between 1977 and 1980. Tariffs were increased on liberalized items
to assist producers to adjust to the removal oi the quantitative restrictions.
Import categories subject to liberalization were primarily in intermediate and
capital goods.  Coverage of final consumer goods and consumer durables remained
largely unchanged, although by 1980 the Ministry of Trade (Comercio) Lad
developed plans for a substantial deepening of the liberalization process.
6.         By 1980, however, expansionary monetary and fiscal policies had begun
to spill over into substantial domestic inflation. Devaluation of the peso was
avoided through increases in oil revenues and foreign borrowing, and as a
result, the real exchange *--e appreciated markedly.  The Government responded
in the area of commercial policy by abandoning the liberalization program for
the second half of the presidential term (sexenio), and by turning progressively
back  toward  the reimposition of quantitative  import controls.   The process
gained momentum in 1981, when tariffs were also increased, and by the time of
the 1982 crisis import controls had been extended to cover 100% of tariff
categories and all of total imports.
Trade Policy and Effective Protection 1960-1982
7.          Table VIII.1 shows the level of effective protection in five broad
industry groups for 1960, 1970, and 1980. In general the structure of effective
protection changed  little in more  than twenty years.   Mexico exhibited  the
pattern of "cascading" effective rates of protection, from very high levels on
final goods to low or negative levels on primary commodities, normally
associated with import substitution regimes, vith the exception that final,
non-durable consumer goods received substantially lower levels of protection
than consumer durable and capital goods.
8.         Effective protection to the primary goods sectors of the economy was
low. In mining it has remained consistently negative, while in agriculture the
price supports introduced under the Sistema Alimenticio Mexicano (SAM) during
the Lopez Portillo administration reversed the trend toward negative effective
protection in agriculture by 1980. Effective protection to non-durable consumer
goods rose slightly between 1960 and 1970 and then declined to its lowest level
in twenty years by 1980. To a large extent this decline reflected very low (and
sometimes negative) levels of effective protection to basic consumer goods, such
as  food  products and  beverages which were available at   subsidized  prices
through the CONASUPO system. However, in a number of important consumer goods
industries such as clothing, cosmetics and pharmaceuticals, effective protection
increased and remained high relative to other consumer goods industries and to
levels of effective protection in general.   Of particular note was the rapid
rise in effective protection to the clothing industry which was heavily
protected by quantitative restrictions on imports.
9.              Intermediate goods  industries experienced a continuous overall
increase in levels of effective protection during the period 1960-1980. For the
most part this reflected a shift in the composition of output toward relatively



- 82 -
MEXICO - EVOLUTION AND REFORM OF                                           ANNEKX VII
TRADE POLICY                                                           Page 3 of 15
more highly protected industries, rather than an increase in levels of effective
protection at the industry level.   Basic chemicals,  rubber products, and non-
metallic minerals were among the activities with the greatest increases in
effective protection rates.   Fertilizers are an interesting case of a reversal
in levels of effective protection, from extremely high in 1970 to negative in
1980.
10.         Durable consumer and capital goods registered the highest overall
effective protection, as well as persistent growth in levels of protection
during the entire period.  Particularly notable increases in levels of protec-
tion occurred in machinery, electrical and electronic machinery and apparatus,
transport equipment and automobiles and parts. In automobile production protec-
tion declined between 1960 and 1970 and then sharply increased during the period
1970-1980.
11.         The overall level and dispersion of effective protection increased
steeply from 1979 to 1981, primarily because of the rapid expansion of the quota
regime (Table VIII.3).  Internal inflation increased more rapidly than external
inflation, with widely differing impacts on implicit nominal protection depend-
ing on the effects of import quotas.  The range of ERPs was the greatest among
import substitution industries, while exportables seem to have had a more
limited spread of protection.   Agriculture generally has negative protection.
The combination of strong price controls in agriculture and increased protection
for import substituting industries accounted for these widening differentials in
protection to various industries.
Export Controls
12.         In 1976, about 797 export positions (fracciones) out of 3,026 were
subject to export controls.  The rationale for quantitative control of exports
was primarily to enforce domestic production and marketing controls.   Export
supply was viewed as the residual of domestic production in excess of
consumption. This general motivation was evident in the types of goods covered
by controls--important basic food items, such as meat and milk, products
regulated by parastatals like sugar and coffee and products with sanitation
regulations, for example seafoods.   By 1980, controls had been reduced to 578
positions, comprising 60% of total exports (about 85% of nonoil exports). Goods
conLsidered as internal market priorities are the major group remaining under
export controls (Table VIII.28).
13.         Export controls, while ensuring that supply of the domestic market
took priority, discouraged production by depressing domestic prices below FOB
equivalents. This effect was reinforced by regulation of price changes and/or
administered pricing which created additional divergences between international
and domestic relative prices. As the regulated pricing system became increas-
ingly misaligned with international relative prices, levels of negative effec-
tive protection increased in primary goods production and the requirement for
strict control of exports increased.
s. INITIAL EFFORTS AT WEFORN: 1983-85
14.        The de Is Madrid Administration took office in late 1982 with two
primary economic policy objectives: first, to stabilize the economy and provide
a viable short- to medium-tern strategy for recovery from the external and



- 83 -
MEXICO - EVOLUTION AND REFORM OF                                           ANNEX VII
TRADE POLICY                                                            Page 4 of 15
internal crises of 1982; and second, to promote longer term structural change in
the Mexican economy with the objective of making it more internationally
competitive  and  less  dependent  on  petroleum  exports.    Both  the  National
Development Plan of 1983 and the sectoral Plan for Industry and Foreign Trade of
1984 emphasized the need to increase the efficiency of Mexican industry through
greater exposure to international competition.
15.      The exigencies of the stabilization program and the perceived political
difficulty  of  initiating  meaningful  trade  liberalization  resulted  in  very
limited action on commercial policy  in 1983.   Coverage  of the quota regime
remained  at 100%  of  total  imports.   The Ministry  of Commerce and Industry
initiated a program to rationalize the tariff structure in order to achieve a
greater uniformity of effective rates of protection based on tariffs alone.
Early implementation efforts, however, primarily involved "ecreasing nominal
tariff rates on a number of intermediate s'd capital goods on which quantitative
restrictions had been reimposed in 1982.
16.      in 1984, the Government moved quite slowly in its reform of commercial
policy and by end 1984, only 17% of total imports had been freed from quanti-
tative controls.   Tariff adjustments continued primarily to consist of donward
adjustments in categories subject to quantitative restrictions.   In early 1985
the Government announced its intention to reduce the coverage of the quota
regime to between 55% and 65% of total imports by the end of the year.   The
Ministry of Commerce and Industry also proposed to alter the structure of tariff
protection to provide transitional support to activities in the sectors to be
liberalized.  Some limited removal of licenses and restructuring of the tariff
schedule was accomplished in the first half of 1985, against substantial opposi-
tion in many segments of the bureaucracy and the private sector, and by end-June
the coverage of import restrictions had been reduced to 75.5% of total imports
(1984 weights). Average tariff rates, weighted by imports, for both controlled
and freed imports were approximately equal at 8.6% and 8.9% respectively.
17.        By 1985, export controls were reduced to about 265 tariff positions,
and about 14.8% of total exports (44.1% of non-oil exports).  The structure did
not substantially change.   Agriculture still accounted for the major share of
the controlled nonoil exports, about 37.8%.   In the manufacturing sector,  the
introduction of voluntary export restraints on steel and steel products led to
the imposition of export controls on these items, but no other major changes
were apparent.   Prior to 1983,  the procedure for applying for permission to
export was quite extensive and tedious; by 1985, this procedure has been
substantially simplified and shortened.
Movements in Nominal Implicit Protection
18.      The magnitude of the 1982 devaluation, coupled with some stickiness in
domestic prices, resulted in widespread negative nominal implicit protection to
import substitution activities throughout 1983.   Internal price controls were
present for about 52.2% of domestic production (1983 values) and significantly
affected nominal protection.  A high incidence of price controls are found in
agriculture and agro-based products, petroleum and deriv. cives, fertilizers,



- 84 -
MEXICO - EVOLUTION SM REFORM OF                                           £13  VII
TRADE POLICY                                                           Pakg  5 of 15
pharmaceuticals, soaps, domestic appliances, and automobiles.  However, except
for a few agricultural commodities, the negative nominal rates were not sus-
tamned in later years as domestic inflation rose faster than international
inflation.
19.       Agricultural support prices in peso,terms were well below their border
price equivalents, resulting in negative protection of most agricultural commod-
ities, which was not reversed until early 1985.  The negative nominal implicit
protection to agriculture was supported by export restrLetions which prevented
export sales of agrlcultural commodities at international prices. Similar pat-
terns of colnciding negative nominal protection and price controls were also
observed in basic consumer goods and some intermediates.   Some of the nonagri-
cultural products with negative protection and extensive price controls are
paper and cardboard, petrochemicals, pharmaceuticals, petroleum and its deriva-
Lives, fertilizer, glass, and basic metals. Home electrical appliances and auto-
mobiles are two sectors where, despite price controls, nominal protection is
still substantially positive.
20.       Positive implicit protection prevailed for some intermediate products,
metal products, and consumer durables, which had very high protection prior to
1982.   Among these heavily protected industries are rubber products, resins,
plastics, machinery and equipment, and household appliances and automobiles.
Thus, although by 1982 quota coverage was comprehensive, the protective effect
of the quota regime was primarily felt in manufactures of heavy intermediate and
consumer durables, sectors in which high rates of protection had prevailed
throughout the post-war period.   Most manufacturing activities benefited from
positive and rising protection due to the quota system. By early 1985, negative
implicit nominal protection prevailed mostly for agriculture, agro-industry, and
oil and oil products, while positive implicit protection was observed for miner-
al products and the major part (about 67Z of production activ ties) of non-food
manufacturing industries. Furthermore, among the manufactured products that had
negative protection were fe-tilizers, pharmaceuticals, and printing, products
that were heavily subjected to price controls.
Movements in Effective Protection
21.       The movements in implicit protection between 1982 and 1985 are reflec-
ted in similar changes in effective protection.   In the period following the
1982 devaluation, levels of effective protection diminished due to the temporary
fall in domestic prices below their peso equivalent international prices. The
reintroduction of quantitative restrictions in 1982, coupled with differing
rates of internal relative price movements, caused an increase in the variance
of effective protection rates. Much of the negative effective protection asso-
ciated with the devaluation and stabilization period had begun to erode by late
1984, and prior to the July 1985 reduction in quantitative restrictions prelimi-
nary estimates show that effective protection rates were rising again to their
historical levels.
22.        Due to the limited change in commercial policy, the structure of ef-
fective protection in Mexico remained largely unchanged between 1982 and mid-
1985.  Primary products, especially agriculture and crude oil, basic consumer
products, and other agro-industrial products, received low or negative effective
protection, while mineral products and most of non-food manufactured products



- 85 -
MEICO - nVOLUTION AND REFORM OF                                           ANNEK VII
TRADE POLICY                                                           Page 6 of 15
received high effective protection.  The highest rates of effective protection
(greater than 50%) were found in capital goods, consumer durables, and import
competing intermediates (Table VIII.5).
C. THE JULY 1985 AND SUBSEQUENT REFORNS
23.         On July 25, 1985 the Government announced the elimination of quota
restrictions on 3604 tariff positions, representing approximately 37% of 1984
imports. This brought the percentage of total imports covered by licenses down
to 38.8%, the lowest level since 1960.  Tariff rates on liberalized Items were
adjusted upward to cushion the fall in relative prices, and the import weighted
average tariffs for controlled and liberalized items diverged substantially at
6.3% and 17.1%, respectively.
Changes in Quntitative Restrictions
24.        Table VIII.6 summarizes the extent of liberalization of quantitative
restrictions  carried out on individual industries.   All lntermediate  goods
industries were heavily liberalized, together with a few consumer goods
products, pharmaceuticals, and selected industrial capital goods. Quantitative
restrictions on consumer durables, notably household electrical appliances and
automobiles, remained unchanged. Limited liberalization was carried out in
agricultural commodities, processed food products, alcoholic beverages, apparel,
and leather products  (See Table VIII.6).   Among manufactured food products,
major changes occurred in animal foods and malt beverages, and minor ones in
nixtomal products and animal and vegetable oils.   Among nondurable consumer
goods, apparel, tobacco, and, to a lesser extent, soap, are still heavily
restricted.   Most capital goods were only partially released from (Rs, with
heavier liberalization in electronic equipment, structural shapes, and metal
furniture-
Changes In the Tariff Structure
25.          Tariff adjustments made following the July reform have somewhat
increased levels of nominal tarlff protection.   Between 1982 and June 1985,
average tariff protection declined from 27% to about 21.7%, and the standard
deviation  from 24.8% to 18.6%.   The July, 1985 reform then increased this
average to about 25.3Z, although the level of dispersion was virtually
unchanged.  The number of tariff rates decreased from 20 rates in 1978 to 10 at
present.
26.       Tariffs were increased to replace QRs in all cases where the QRs were
binding (when nominal implicit protection exceeded tariff protection) and
increased slightly when implicit protection was positive but less than the
tariff.  Tariffs remained unchanged in most cases when the initial protection
was negative with the exception of forestry, basic chemicals, glass products,
animal foods and other manufacturing (see Table VIII.6).
27.         In cases where the QR was binding, it appears that nominal tariffs
were raised to provide the levels of the implicit protection prior to July,
1985. For example poultry, meat and mllk products, resins, plastics, nonelectri-
cal machinery, electrical machinery, and chemicals had implicit nominal protec-
tion rates under the quota regime (141, 30Z, 23%, 431, 181, 48%, and 29%,



- 86 -
MEXICO - EVOLUTION AM  REFORM OF                                         ANNEX VII
TRADE POLICY                                                          Page 7 of 15
respectively) whlch exceeded the level of tariff protection. In each case, upon
removal of import controls, tariff rates were raised to equal approximately the
levels of implicit protection.
Official Reference Prices
28.           Following the July 25 reductions of quantitative restrictions,
official reference prices were extended from 4.4% to 7.6% of total 1984 imports,
and from 749 to 1,215 tariff positions.  By April 1986, this coverage decreased
to 7% of  1984 imports, and to 1,177 tariff positions.   As of March  1986
reference prices covered 17.5Z of production, 12.5% of which are on prodLcts
with no quantitative restrictions. While official reference prices were origin-
ally used for exchange control and antidumping purposes, there is some evidence
to indicate that they are now used for protectionist purposes. For example, in
the second half of 1985, official prices were adjusted or imposed only for items
that had been freed from import controls.  Preliminary estimates from a sample
of 1189 products with official prices show that, on average, Implicit protection
for commodities under reference prices is 36.8% higher than the nominal protec-
tion permitted by the tariff rates alone.   Within the sample, the effect on
implicit protection of reference prices is widely dispersed (Table VIII.19).
Nominal and Effective Protection
29.       Preliminary estimates of implicit nominal protection since July, 1985
show a reversal in its rising trend. The average fell to -11.O in July, -20.3%
in August, and declined further to -22.4% in December 1985.  The peso devalua-
tion of about 70% during this period, coupled with the remaining price and quota
controls, largely explains the fall in overall implicit protection.  Since the
majority of Mexico's productive sectors continue to be regulated by quotas and/
or domestic price controls, It is not surprising that overall implicit protec-
tion declined. The decline, however, is transitional and will be eroded in the
same fashion as that experienced between 1982 and 1984.
30.        One of the more dramatic changes in early 1986 was the relaxation of
price controls on tortillas and corn products, and the release of a plan to do
the same for bread, wheat products, and other basic foodstuffs. Domestic price
controls have been mainly responsible for negative protection for these
industries.
31.          Effective protection rates also declined after July 1985 (Table
VIII.8).  Overall, average effective protection dropped from -6.0Z in January-
June to -102 in July, and to -19.1% in August 1985. The average EPR appears to
have remained at about -20% until the end of 1985. The degree to which effective
protection rates declined differed among commodities, and some commodities
Lctually had increased protection.   Agriculture and other products with price
controls had negative effective protection. Capital goods, a few intermediates,
and consumer durables retained their positive effective protection.  Thus, high
protection basically remained for most of nonfood manufactures, especially for
consumer durables.
32.           The extensive liberalization of import permits in mid-1985 was
strongly biased against intermediate goods and toward consumer durables and
capital goods (Table VIII.22).  There is some indication that while effective



- 87 -
MEXICO - EVOLUTION AND REFORM OF                                           ANNEX VII
TRADE POLICY                                                            Page 8 of 15
protection declined for almost all items after the July devaluation, it declined
more for intermediate goods than for capital goods, consumer durables, and some
agro-based products that remained controlled by import licenses (Table VIII.-
22).   Relatively small decreases In EPRs were observed for wheat and agricul-
tural products, primarily because of changes made to domestic prices by the
government. Protection of consumer durables such as automobiles and domestlc
appliances and some capital goods also seems not to have declined substantial-
ly. Large decreases were observed for some intermediate products, such as
resins, basic chemicals, basic metals and metal products, and consumer noadura-
bles like meat and milk products. Interesting cases of increased protection,
despite the devaluation, were found in coffee processing, animal fats, cement,
and plastics (whose EPR fell in mid-1985 but recovered in December). Protection
remained unchanged for nonferrous metals and clay and stone. The trend seems to
show a greater reduction of protection for Intermediates, than for agriculture
'nd consumer durables.
33.       The dispersion of effective protection decreased between 1982 and mid-
1985, and increased substantially, because of the dcvaluation, in the second
half of 1985. While dispersion of nominal protection has reversed to predevalu-
ation levels, the dispersion of effective protection has not which implies that
the liberalization may partially account for an increased disparity in incen-
tives in favor of some capital goods, consumer durables, and automobiles.   A
large part of the disparity in incentives seems to be accounted for by the pre-
sence of some goods whose domestic price controls lead to highly negative
protection. The more prominent of these goods are corn, wheat anid bread, torti-
llas, sugar, animal feeds, beverages, fertilizers and pharmaceuticals. If these
goods are excluded total dispersion decreases dramatically (and average effec-
tive protection rises as well).   Goods with historically very high protection
such as meat and milk, synthetic fibers, other chemicals, plastics, electric
appliances, and automobiles also seem to have wide fluctuations in their effec-
tive protection rates from one period to another, primarily due to differential
movements and domestic on international prices.
D.  THE PRESENT STATUS OF THE TRADE POLICY REGIME
34.       Since July 1985 chere has been a continuous process of gradual libera-
lization of the trade policy regime.  This section summarizes the Vnesent status
of tariff and non-tariff restrictions on trade in Mexico.
Quantitative Restrictions and Other Non-7briff Barriers
35.        There are still 816 tariff positions under quantitative restrictions,
accounting for 38.5% of the value of 1984 imports (in comparison to 83% before
the July, 1985 reforms) (Table VIII.2).   An estimated 55% of 1983 production
(43.4% of nonoil production) and 50 of final demand are protected by import
licenses (Table VIII.13). Of the 38.5% of total imports remaining under quanti-
tative restrictions, 20.4% of total imports is accounted for by agriculture and
forestry and 7.4Z by capital goods and automobiles. The majority of controlled
tariff positions require import licenses (permiso previo), while imports for 311
tariff positions are automatically banned (negativa automatica).



- 88 -
MEXICO - EVOLUTION AND REFORM OF                                           ANEX VII
TRADE POLICY                                                           ftPge 9 of 15
36.         Coverage of the quota regime is neither uniform across sectors nor
among types of goods.   Figure VIII.14 illustrates the coverage of value added
among sectors and by types of manufactured goods.  Oil and agriculture have the
highest incidence  of  production  coverage  of  import  quotas.   Manufacturing,
although less heavily protected, continues to have 441 of total value added
protected by import controls.   Within the manufacturing sector the changes in
July, 1985 reduced the production coverage of import permits more for Intermedi-
ate and capital goods than for consumer goods. Approximately 70% of the produc-
tion of consumer goods and 981 of the production of consumer durables remain
under import control.   As Figure VIII.4 illustrates, the production coverage
dimlnishes substantially for capital goods, light and heavy intermediates.
37.       The disparities between production coverage and import coverage of the
quota regime are apparent from Table VIII.14.   In manufacturing quantitative
restrictions account for a substantially larger share of production than imports
for consumer goods, consumer durables and capital goods.  In these sectors the
quantitative restrictions are nearly prohibitive of imports.   In intermediate
goods, on the other hand, import and production weights coincide more closely,
as a result of less restrictive controls on competing imports.
38.         A comprehensive estimate of the coverage of non-tariff barriers to
imports in Mexico can be obtained by combining the estimates of coverage of
import quotas and official reference prices.   Official reference prices cur-
rently are imposed on product groups accounting for approximately 17.3% of
production. of these product groups 5.0% of production is also covered by quan-
titative import restrictions, while 12.3Z is subject to reference prices alone.
Since about 55% of production is covered by quota restrictions, about 67.8% of
total productioi. is subject to non-tariff barriers to trade.
Nominal Tariff Rates
39.      The reforms in July replaced quantitatlve restrlctions with tariffs as
the major instrument for protection, primarily to increase the transparency of
the protection structure.   Thus, together with the reductions in quantitative
restritions, the government undertook a series of adjustments in the tariff
rates.  The average tariff rate weighted by 1984 import values increased from
8.6% in early 1985 to 12.4%.  Products which have been freed from QRs have an
import weighted average of 17.4% (compared to 9.41 prior to July, lA85), while
those still under QRs have an average of 4.5% (compared to the pre-July average
of 7.85). Production weighted average tariffs also increased from 18% in early
1985 to 22% in late October.  The standard deviation of tariff rates decreased
from 22.5% in early 1985 to  18.91.   Prior to the replacement of QRs with
tariffs, 50% of the tariff rates were between 0 and 15%; now, they fall within 0
to 25%. The number of tariff positions with rates above 75% also fell.
40.        Among the various sectors, manufacturing has a higher average tariff
than agriculture or minerals. Finished consumer goods also have higher tariffs
than intermediate or capital goods.   Tariff rates of around 101 dominate for
capital and intermediate goods not produced or only partially produced in
Mexico, between 25% and 40% for all other industrial products, and from 50 to
100% for "unnecessary" consumer goods.  Across industries, malt and alcoholic



- 89 -
MEXICO - EVOLUTION AND REFORM OF                                           ANNEX VII
TRADE POLICY                                                           Page 10 of 15
beverages, selected textiles, garments, leather and shoes, cosmetics, glass
products, electronic equipment, automobiles, and miscellaneous have tariff
averages greater than 50%.
Export Mazes and Controls
41.          As of July 31, 1985, export controls were imposed on 265 export
classes (fracciones), accounting for 14.4% of total exports and 44% of non-oil
exports, while export taxes, or a combination of taxes and controls, were
imposed on 171 export classes.  In October, 1985, 15 fracciones were freed from
the controlled list, but these were minor exports and the removal did not signi-
ficantly change the export coverage of the restrictions. The administering
institutions for export taxes are SECOFI, the Ministry of Fisheries, and the
various parastatals which canalize the exports of their respective products.
42.         The bulk of the QRs on exports apply to three broad categories: (1)
ccmmodities for which Mexico has to abide by international quota agreements,
such as coffee, tobacco, cotton, and some minerals.  These products account for
about 4.8% of all exports; (2) products such as processed food, seafood,
pharmaceuticals, iron and steel products, and petroleum derivatives, where
export controls have been introduced either for reasons of quality and price
controls, and/or because of the voluntary export restraint agreement with the
United States. These products represent about 5.2% of Mexico's exports; and (3)
agricultural products in which Mexico's domestic production is considered
deficient (staples such as corn, soybeans, sorghum, and flour), for which
domestic prices are well below world market prices and where there are monopoly
marketing and distribution channels (e.g. CONASUPO and/or some other parastatal
enterprises). This category accounts for 3.4% of total exports.
43.         Export controls are, therefore, linked with the presence of pricing,
marketing, and bilateral trade policies, and liberalization of such controls
would entail changes in these  complementary policies.   In agriculture, for
example, liberalization would depend on the reform of domestic pricing policies
and distribution channels. Among manufactures, public sector efforts at quality
control would have to be replaced by private initiative and international
agreements would have to change to eliminate fully export restrictions.
Sector Program. (Programas de Ram)
44.         One of the key omissions of the current trade policy reform is the
continued use of trade restricting measures in industrial sector promotion
programs.  Sector programs for industrial promotion with built-in protectionist
measures, such as domestic content requirements and quantitative restrictions on
competing imports, have historically been used in Mexico.   One of the major
features of the existing industrial programs (programas de rama) is an incentive
and regulatory package designed to encourage increased use of inputs produced
domestically, while simultaneously increasing production and exports.  The set
of incentives used 'oy these programs include fiscal incentives, import permits,
and credit preferences.   So far, automobiles  (implemented in August, 1984),
pharmaceuticals  (February,  1984),  and  capital  goods  (September,  1981) have
industrial programs.  A program for electronic products has been designed, but
it is still not approved.



- 90 -
MEXICO - EVOLUTION AND REFORM OF                                           ANNEX VII
TRAlDE POLICY                                                          Page 11 of 15
45.        Under the automotive sector program, firms producing automobiles will
purchase 50% of the value of their parts from the local autoparts industry
starting in 1986. Also, the autoparts industry has a domestic content require-
ment of 50 to 60%.  By 1987, cars will have domestic content of 60%, buses 70%,
heavy transport  80%, and construction transport  90%.   The domestic content
requirement (whose minimum is 33%) diminishes, however, the larger the percen-
tage of the output sold in the external market.  Final output of the sector is
protected by quantitative restrictions on competing imports.   Domestic content
requirements are also imposed on capital goods (about 50%) and pharmaceuticals.
Importation of competing pharmaceutical products is prohibited under the terms
of the program.  The still pending industrial program for electronics includes
domestic content requirements (of 55% for microcomputers and 45% for mini-
computers) within three years of the implementation of the program. Other
electronic equipment or supplies must have a 35-70% domestic content (depending
on the type of product) at the end of the same three year period. The program
also gives lmport permits only for inputs not produced domestically and
guarantees a procurement policy of the government that is biased toward
domestically produced electronic products.
46.         Recent discussions with the authorities charged with development of
the sectoral programs indicate, however, that future sectoral programs will be
designed around  the  use  of  more  transparent  promotional  instruments.    At
present, the other sectoral programs under development are being designed to use
tariff  protection  of  domestic  production  as  the  principal  promotional
instrument, and there is some indication that existing programs may be revised
to increase the transparency of protection.
Rule Eight (Regla Octava)
47.         Producers covered by the industrial promotion programs of tte Trade
and Industry Ministry may avail themselves of thre benefits pruvided by Rule
Eight. These priority industries are granted the right to import their inputs
at a tarifff rate of 5%.  The impact of rule eight is to increase effective
protection for industries producing for the domestic market. For industries
under the industrial programs, the rule offsets part of the firms' losses from
conforming with the domestic content requirement.   The net effect on effective
protection to the affected firms and products is not measurable with existing
data.
The Proposed Foreign Trade law
48.         The proposed Foreign Trade Law, which was submitted to Congress in
November, will provide the first comprehensive legislative framework for trade
policy instruments and policies that are currently in use by the Executive
branch of the Government and for various individual laws that deal with aspects
of trade  policy.   It will also  provide a legal framework for anti-dumping
measures and countervailing duties that will, upon implementation, replace
official reference prices.



- 91 -
MEXICO - EVOLUTION AND REFORM OP                                         ANNU VII
TRADE POLICY                                                         Page 12 of 15
49.        The impact of the proposed law will depend on its actual administra-
tion and implementation, particularly because of its generality, and the few
limits it sets on the use of trade policy measures by the executive body.  In
particular, implementing instruments will need to distinguish between injury to
domestic producers caused by competitive international prices and unfair trade
practices, an area in which the legislation itself is unclear.
E. THE GOVERNMENT'S PROGRAM OF TRADE POLICY REFORM
50.         The discussion above shows that despite substantial reform of the
trade policy regime, Mexico continues to rely to a considerble degree on
discretionary policies to restrict imports and exports; quantitative restric-
tions on imports and exports cover a substantial part of production. Official
reference  prices  are  also  used  as  another  instrument  to  reduce  import
competition.   Tariffs, thus, still are not firmly established as the primary
instrument  for  trade  regulation.    Furthermore,  protection  is  liuked with
domestic price control policies and  selective industrial programs that impose
domestic content requirements in exchange for some fiscal incentives and import
permitB.
51.        The impact of this trade regime on the system of incentives is a lack
of transparency and a bimodal distribution of protection. Negative nominal and
effective protection prevails for agricultural products, while highly positive
protection is given to some consumer and capital goods and to consumer
durables.  The dispersion and levels of effective protection rose from 1979 to
1982  as  effective  protection  increased  for  selected  import  substituting
industries.  Since then, effective protection levels have fallen because of the
overall effect of the series of devaluations coupled with quantity and price
controls on tradeable goods. However, because of the different impacts of deval-
uation on implicit nominal prutection of various industries, disparities in
effective protection did not decline as much, and the high variance in effective
protective rates, characteristic of the trade policy regime, was preserved. The
following section provides a set of guidelines for policy reforms that are
directed toward increasing the transparency in the incentive system and toward
reducing the level and dispersion of protection across various industries.
Elimination of Semaining Qmutitative Restrictions of Imports
52.       Further trade policy reform, as described in the main text and in the
Statements of Trade Policy (Annex IV), will endeavor to eliminate in a phased
way the remaining quantitative restrictions on imports. The thirty year history
of quantitative restrictions as the major instrument of trade policy in Mexico
suggests that further reform of the quota system may encounter substantial
resistance from affected sectors. About 56.4% of total imports (1983 values)
have continuously been subjected to QRs, since the 1960s. A look at the tariff
positions that have been subjected to QRs for an extended time period shows
that, across product groups, agriculture accounts for the highest percentage of
imports (1983 values) that have been historically subjected to QRs, about
19.2%. The other categories with high historical levels of quota protection are
petrochemicals, chemicals, sugar, non-electric and electric machinery and
equipment, and transport equipment.



- 92 -
taICo  - nLoUTION AND Imo    01F                                          Anz VII
TRADE POLICY                                                          Page 13 of 15
53.         The current coverage of quantitative restrictions is much less than
the coverage of long-standing protected industries given above.  The critical
sectors resain the same however, notably agriculture, petrochemicals and other
petroleum derivatives, capital goods, and consumer durables.   (Tables VIII.13
and VIII.15 show the existing shares of these sectors in the total percentage of
production and imports still under Import controls.)
Reductlon In the Dispersion of Nomimal and Effective Protection
54.        As quantitative restrictions are replaced by equivalent tariffs, the
simplest way to reduce the dispersion of effective protection Is to reduce the
dispersion of the nominal tariffs, as the Government has decided to do.   The
Government intends to limit the range of tariffs to within narrower limits,
i.e., both maximum and minimum tariff limits ¶aave been set for each stage of the
program.
55.       A policy simulation can be undertaken which shows the effect of intro-
ducing a lower dispersion of tariffs on the level and dispersion of nominal and
effective protection. In the table below the mean and standard deviation of the
tariff structure, based on 1983 weights, is estimated for the early 1986 tariff
range of 0-100Z, for a range of 0-502, and for four possible end-target ranges.
Two of these end targets have a minimum tariff of 1OZ, with maximum tariff rates
of 30% and 40%, while the others retain the present 0 duty categories of the
tariff structure.

dard deviation of the tarLffs across tariff positions) of 3.34% and mean of
11.0%. The range 10-40 has a higher dispersion of 8.75 and the highest average
tariff, but a substantially lower coefficient of variation than options main-
taining the minimum rate at 0%. The higher mean in the range 10-40% indicates
the relative importance (in total production) of the tariff positions with
tariffs of less than 10% compared to those with tariff rates greater than 40%.
57.       The impact of these scenarios on the structure of effective protection
cannot be readily established, given the extensive protection provided by the
quota system, and price controls. If, however, the absence of any binding quan-

58.       The tariff range that has the lowest average effective protection rate
and standard deviation is 0-30%, which is the one chosen by the Government
(Annex IV), followed closely by the range 10-30%.  The dispersion changes only
slightly, from 14.0X to 16.1%, as one moves from the range 0-30Z to 10-40%, but
the average EPR changes from 25.61 to 33.3%. This is evident from Figure 2.5,
which shows a shift of a basically unchanged distribution to the right between
the two acenarios.  The increase in the average EPR reflects the importance of
the iteus whose nominal tariffs increased to 10%. Due to the structure of nomi-
nal protection, average EPRs and their dispersion decline as the upper limit Is
reduced from 100% to 30% while maintaining the lower limit at O. Moving from
the range 0-40% to 10-40Z hardly alters the average average EPR, but reduces
dispersion, which shows that there was an even distribution of increases to 10%
on inputs and outputs.
Reforu of Official Reference Prices and Sectoral Programs
59.        Official reference prices which have been set to monitor antidumping
practices will be phased out and replaced by a set of implementing rules of
Mexico's new Foreign Trade Law (see Annex IV and sain text). Of paramount impor-
tance to the successful implementation of these rules will be the need clearly
to distinguish between unfair trade practices and low international prices which
do not arise from dumping. The law provides for an injury test which should be
applied in all cases, and countervailing duties should be specific to the
product and country in question.
Reforming Export Controls
60.       Export controls have shown reduced coverage since the 1970's. At this
point, the majority of the existing export controls may be removed only if
changes are made to the domestic price controls and domestic marketing arrange-
ments (e.g. the use of parastatals as conduit between producers and buyers),
otherwise they have to coexist for the domestic controls to be feasible.   The
current liberalization of tortillas, and the potential one for bread and other



- 94 -
MEXICO - EVOLUTION AND  REFORM OF                                        ANME VII
TADE POLICY                                                          frg  15 of 15
foodstuffs, are avenues for reform of export controls in wheat and corn products
and basic foodstuffs. Export controls in Industry as well are heavily linked to
domestic price controls, with the exception of fibers and steel where controls
are imposed to ensure compliance with quotas imposed by international
agreements.
